king Antigonus? What moved Bias to withstand the force of king Aliates. In
fine what made Phocion, Aristides, Themistocles and others to become
soldiers, to stand in arms against their enemies, the love of Athens. The
pilgrimage of this our life is nothing else, but a continual trauaill until we
come to our last journey, which is death, then is the end of all pilgrimage,
and just accoumpt to be made for the same.

Of Dissimulation and Craft, of Subtlety and Deceipte.
Diogenes.
DIogenes that Cinike Philosopher, making him self ignorant sometime in that,
which he knew best was wont in banquettes and feasts, to say if any manne
had demanded what kind of meat, were there I can not name, but I ca^ eat it,
and so would pass to answer any thing truly with dissimulatio^s, in so much
that Sigismonde the Emperor would say, that he that could not dissemble,
Galba.
could not rule. At what time Galba a Citizein of Rome, had bidden a gentleman
named Mecaenas unto Supper, perceiuyng the gentleman to be in love with his
wife, fained him a sleep, for that Mecaenas might show some part of his will,
and love in the mean season, Mecaenas.
while his wife and Mecaena were in talk, and he him self in dissemblyng
slept, came one of his servants, to take some things away from the table,
supposyng his master had been a sleep, unto whom his master said, well you
varlet, though I see not Mercaenas, yet I see to you, I sleep unto him, and
not unto you. The like dissimulation was between Demosthenes and Archias,
Demosthenes Archias.
at what time he fled Athens, for fear of Antipaters displeasure, and went
unto the Isle Calauria, where in the Temple of Neptune he hid him self until
Archias came, and promised him what honor and dignity he should have, if he
could come unto Antipater, and with fair words he dissembled with
Demosthenes, that he came for the purpose from the king unto him, Demosthenes
perceiuyng his dissimulations, and crafty means, answered plainly, to move
him unto anger, where truth is oftentimes opened, and said: Thou of all men
could never play upon the stages, plaiyng thy part then, and now at this
time, thou can not be an Orator to persuade me, whereat Archias waxed
angry, and threatened to hale him out of the Temple, unto whom Demosthenes
answered. Now perforce thy dissimulation is chaunsed unto truth. I might
hereon stay, to note the great dissimulations between Metellus and Scipio,
which was so great that Metellus feigned that Rome was happy, Metellus
dis^imulation.
that Scipio was borne therein, and yet his mortal enemy all the days of his
life, and therefore Frederick an Emperor sometime of Rome, at what time the
Senators would go sit about the state of the city, would say, before you
go unto the Senate house, cast away from you two things that you carry with
you, and being demanded of the Senators, what two things were they, he
said, Simulations, and Dissimulations. In this Philippe of Macedon did differ
much from his soonne Alexander, Alexander,
in so much that Alexander would consent to nothing, but to magnimitie and
truth, and his father to all kind of falsehood, as seen by subduyng of the
Sarunsians, and the Cities of Thracia, who under color of peace, commanded
his Soldiers to bring under their clokes, every one a coarde, Philippe.
that at what time king Philippe made silence to speak, the enemies being
attentife to hear, he retched for the his right hand, for a watch word to
his Soldiers, suddenly to bind with their coardes their enemies, and to
bring them captives to Macedonia. The like craft used Alcibiades amongst the
Agrigentines, Alcibiades.
fainyng that he had to speak for the common profit, as well of Athens, as
of Agrigentu^, calling the^ in place, as though he would open some thing
necessary unto them, had the Greeks ready in the mean time to take the city,
and to possess their substance by this craft. Such craft used Thrasillus,
to take the City Byzantium, such deceipte used Zopirus, to overcome Babylon.
Tarquinius.
Such did Tarquinius the soonne of Sextus Tarquinius practice against the
Gabians, who when he perceived, that his father might by no means subdue them,
he imitated Zopirus craft, making the enemies to believe that he was ill
handeled, and cruelly used of his father, and that he knew well how to deceive
his father, and to betraie him unto them, they being ready to believe
Tarquinius, made him chief of their company: he straight sent to his father
messengers, to signify unto him, that he might do his pleasure with his
enemies. The old Tarquinius understanding the craft, and subtlety of his
soonne, brought the messenger unto a fair garden, mistrustyng like a wise
prince the matter, gave this subtle warning unto his sons ambassador.
Walkyng up and down the Garden with diverse noble men, he with his staff
beat the chief flowers of the Garden, saying unto the messenger fare well,
tell my soonne what I do, and bid him do accordyngly, which young Tarquinius
perceiuyng his fathers mind, slew the best of the enemies, oppressed the chief
men, and betrayed the City unto his father. By this means the craft that
Conon the Athenian, Conon.
deceived the Persians in Ciprus. The subtlety that Pysistratus used, to
begile the people of Megaera, and Haniball in Italy are of like effect, that
subdued Tarentum, in so much that Hanibal was wo^t to say, when the Romans
had again won Tarentu^. Eadem art qua prius ^aepimus Tarentum amifimus: For
by craft Hanibal vanquished the Tarentines, and by craft did the Romans win
the same again. Antigonus deceived the Citizeins of Corinth, Antigonus.
under the color of marriage, betwixt his soonne Demetrius, and Alexanders
wife, who then was a widow, and a Queen in Corinth, that in the midst of
triumphs, and preparations to the marriage, Antigonus by deceipte took the
Castle, commanded his soldiers in arms, and proclaimed him self king in
Corinth. In the same book of Poliaenus, the like history is written of
Lysander of Sparta, Lysander.
and Nearchus of Crete, the one promisyng to the inhabitauntes of Miletum, his
aid and help, in defending their liberties, the people giuyng credit unto a
kings promise, trusted to have Lisander their special friend, they found him
their mortal foe, for he deceived them thereby, and took the city of
Miletum unto him self. The other sailyng unto the Haven of Telmessus, to renew
friendship with Antipatridas, who then governed the city of Telmessus, under
the color of frendshippe, he had his men of arms ready on the Sea, to
destroy his friend, to take the city unto him self. This deceit was not
only seen in wars, where much falshoode and perjury are practiced, but in
all things men use craft, according to the proverb, There is craft in
daubyng. To speak of Theodectes craft toward his master Aristotle, to
spoil him privily of his glory. To speak of Sertorius deceipte, in winnyng
authority among the common people. To describe the means that Dionisius used
to get money amongst the Siracusans, or how Pythius deceived Cannius in his
bargain of Fish. Or how Darius became king of Persia, by nisyng of a Mare and
a million more such deceiptes and crafts, I will that the reader read
Poliaenus, when he shall have enough of falshode, because craft is used
diversely, I will somewhat touch those that used craft, in altering the^ selves
in form, and shape of women, some for filthy lust, some for virtue sake,
some for vice. What kind of dissimulation was in Sardanapalus King of Si^ia,
Sardanapalis.
to forsake the Empire, to forgo his kingdom, to become from a Prince, like a
woman to spinne and card with his concubines, and so from the shape of a man,
to dessemble him to be a woman. What kind of dissimulation did that renowned,
even the ofspryng of Gods, and soonne unto Jupiter, Hercules.
that mighty Hercules, after that he tamed monsters, slew Giantes, overcame
Dragons, Lions, wild beasts, and yet to translate him from a champion and a
conqueror, in a woman's apparel, formed him self a woman, with such cautell
and crafty dissimulations, that he served Omphale Queen of Lidia like a woman,
in the apparel of a woman at the whele, at the cards, at Omphales
commandment. What kind of craft used Clodius, to bring his purpose to
pass, with Pompeia, Caesar's wife likewise dissemblyng him self to be a woman,
as Cicero tanteth him in an epistle that he writes unto Lentulus, where he
says that Clodius dissembled with the Nymph Bona dea, as he was wont to use
the three sisters Thus Clodius would at all times go unto Pompeia, Clodius.
in the apparel of a woman to use such feats, that made Caesar to deuorse his
wife Pompeia. Dissimulations, cautelles, crafts, as they are most evil to
practice wicked things, so are they of the contrary, most necessary to do
good, Euclides.
as Euclides, which used the like craft as before, but to the better purpose,
where they practiced this feate, to feed lust, to pleasure affection, he used
it to see Socrates, read Philosophy, and to learn wisdom, for there was a
Law between Athens and the Megaris, for the great hatered that the one bare
unto the other, that who so ever came from Athens unto Megaris, should die. And
who so ever would go from Megaris unto Athens should likewise die. This death
feared not Euclides so much from his purpose, but love that he bare unto
Socrates, unto Philosophy, and unto wisdom, was as much that he would in the
night travail from Megaris unto Athens. In the apparel of a woman, lest he
should be known, and return before day from Athens, unto Megaris again.
This dissimulation and craft of Euclides was far better, and more to be
commended, then the doings of the fore renowned Princes. Semiramis. Iusti.
lib. i.
Better is Semiramis Queen of Babylon thought of that she perceiving her young
son Ninus, to be too tender to govern the stout Babilonians and Assirians,
knowing the nature of the people to be impaciente of a woman's gouernemente,
she became in apparel like a man, and rule the kingdom, until her son came
unto ripe age. More praise ought Pelagia have a woman of Antioch though she
fained him self to be a man, Pelagia.
and dissembled with the world in that case, yet this was to avoid pleasure
and lust, and to live chastely and solitary, without the company of men.
Marina.
For this cause is the Greek virgin Marina, and Euphrosina a maid of
Alexandria worthy preferred before Cleocritus and Clisthenes, Euphrosina.
for that they went in the apparel of men, Clisthenes.
to live in the wilderness, to avoid lust and sensuality: the others went
in the apparel of women, to begile women. Caelius does report certain women
as Mantinea, Lasthenia, Axiothea, and Phliasia would come in their apparel
like men, Phliasia.
to hear Plato read Philosophy in school. The cause of their dissimulations
was virtue and honest life: The cause of the others crafts and dissembling
feats, was vice and ill life, so that dissimulation is either good, or bad.
For we read at what time the launcyng laddes of Greece, had determined to
fetch home again fair Helene Menelaus wife from Troie, where she was
ravished by Paris king Priamus soonne, that then Achilles the stoutest, and
worthieste of all the Greeks, while yet he slepte in the tente of Chiron his
mother Thetis, Chiron.
which the poets fain to be a Goddess, suddenly took him from Chirons house,
Thetis
and changed his apparel like a woman, and appoincted where he should hide him
self with the daughters of king Lycomedes, where he got of one of them with
child, which was Pyrrhus, which was Deidamia soonne, and commanded him to
betraie him to no man, for she knew that her son Achilles should die in
Troie, Achilles
if he would go thither. There Achilles a long while, at the commandment of
his mother Thetis, was until the oracle was given that the city of Troie
should never be destroyed without the help of Achilles, Vlixes.
Vlixes being most subtle and crafty, took upon him to seek out Achilles,
took a little coffer full of fine wares meet for women, Dissimulation
and a strong bow and arrows, which when Vlixes came unto king Lycomedes
daughters, knowing Achilles to be there, but because he was in the apparaill
of a woman, he knew him not, and therefore showed his fine ware unto the
kings daughters, a strong bow bente by him, while Deidamia and the rest of
her sisters, viewed the glisteryng ware of Vlixes, Achilles stepte by, and
took Vlixes bow in hand, and drew it, whereby Vlixes straight perceived, by
the drawing of so strong a bow, that he was Achilles, and thus one craft
beguileth an other, one deceipte deceives an other, and one dissemblyng manne
finds out an other, for by this means of crafty Vlixes, was the
dissimulation of Achilles known. I might have just occasion here, to speak
of those that were much given unto soft clothyng, Aristotle.
gaie apparaill, and delicate fare, as Aristotle the Prince of Philosophers,
delited to go brave in gorgious apparel, with chains and ringes, and had
herein great felicity. Hortenfius.
Demosthenes and Hortenfius, two famous and noble Orators, the one of Athens,
the other of Rome, went so trim and fine, in their clothes, with such neat
and wanton gesture, that L. Torquatus would often call Hortensius Dionisias
soonne, for that she had great pleasure in dauncyng, and gestures of bodies,
but I will omit such, and speak of dissemblyng persons, which thought to
hurt others, destroyed them selves, as that strong Golias contempnyng all
Israel in force and strength, Judith a selie woman overcame him. Hammon was
hanged upon that galowes, that he prepared for Mardocheus, even Absalon going
about to destroy his father king David, hanged by the hears of his head, by
Gods appoinctement.

Of Famine.
CICERO in his first book of Tuscilane questions does note the saying of
Socrates, that hounger was the best sauce for meat, and thirst the best
occasion to drink. Wherefore King Dionisius the tyrant, hearing much report
of the Lacedemonia^s fare, and specially of their Poddage, which was called
Ius nigrum, the black Poddage, bought a Cook of the country as her servant,
to diet him in the ordinary fare of the Lacedaemonians, which after much
pain taken of the Cook, in making these foresaid Poddage was had, he brought
a measse thereof unto the king, which sore longed for it, which as soon as he
tasted of it, he powered it again out of his mouth very angry unto the
Cook, saying: Is this the famous fare and Princely Poddage, that the
Lacedaemonians so much brag of, my dog should not, said Dionisius, eat this,
the Cook perceiuyng the glottonie, and excess fare of the king, said: O
Dionisius when so ever thou eat of this Poddage, thou must bring fit sauce for
this meat, which is Lacedaemonians stomach, for the Princes of Sparta have
more pleasure in this kind of fare, then ever king Midas had in his golden
banquettes. What makes any meat sweet, hounger? Or what causes man to feed
pleasauntly, hounger? What causes any drink pleasant, Darius.
thirst? For at what time Darius enforced of mere thirst to drink of a
lake, all defiled with stinkyng carkases of dead soldiers, Ciceronis. lib. ^.
Tusc.
being then in wars, and compelled then to take his flight, he said after
his draught, that he never drank sweter drink in his life. Though this king
was a proud prince over the Persians, and had all kind of wines at
commandment, yet his excess and abundance then, his want and penury now,
his small stomach then, and his thurstie stomach now, was the only cause of
this noble drink, which he so much commended, and preferred before all the
wine that ever he drank before. Even so reported king Artaxerxes in his
wars, Artaxerxes.
when his victuales, Brusonius. lib. ii. Cap. xli.
and all were spoiled by the enemies, of few dry Figs, and of a piece of a
Barley loffe, upon which he fed so hongerly, that he spoke after this sort.
O good Lord, of how great a pleasure have I been this while ignoraunte.
Lisimachus.
Lisimachus like wise being in wars in Thracia, Domicianus the Emperor,
where he and all his soldiers were kept so long without drink, until he
was so thurstie, that he was enforced and all his host, to yield as captives
to the Emperor Domitianus, and now being in captivity, having a draught of
drink of the Emperor, he said: O GOD that I should make my self from a king
to be a captive from a noble prince of Greece, to be a bond slave unto the
Romans, for one draught of drink. Se what honger and thirst is? how it has
made kings to yield, princes to be vanquished. Yea, and has made King
Ptholomie in his own kingdom and country kingdom of Aegipte, Ptholemie.
to commend a piece of bread, which was given him in a poor cottage, to say
that he never eat better meat, nor more comfortable cheer in his life, thou
that piece of bread was. It was the very order of that noble Emperor Julius
Caesar in all his wars, Julius Caesar.
more with famine, then with sword, to vanquishe his enemies: For this famous
warrier would often say, that even as the Physician would use his pacie^tes,
so would he use the enemies. The rule of ye physician is, to make his paciente
fast to recover his health. The order of Caesar was, to keep the enemies from
victual, to make them yield. Great is the force of famine, as by histories
we read, Cambyses.
that when king Cambises marchyng toward the Aethiopians, had fallen unto
scarceness of victualles, and unto such penury, and want of food unto the
soldiers, that they agreed within them selfs to kille the tenthe throughout
all the host, to assuage honger, in so much that it continued so long, that
Cambises the king was in great fear, lest the lotte should at length happen
upon him, and so to be eaten of his soldiers. Saguntus a City in Spain,
Saguntus.
sometime as Eutropius does witness, in great amity with the Romans, and
being besieged of the Carthagineans so long, that all the city was brought
unto such famine, that the lords and the captains of the city, made a great
fire in the Markette place, and there brought all their wealth and substance,
and threw it into the fire, and after made their wives, and then their
children to be burned, and last of all the chief lords and capitaines, ended
their lives in flaming fire, lest they should come unto the enemies hand, so
great was that famine, that it was before Prognosticated by a woman brought to
bed, whose child as soon as he was borne, entered unto his mothers womb
again. The like calamity happened in Caligurium, a City where Quintilian was
borne, who being likewise long besieged of Cn. Pompeius, to bring them in
subjection and to keep promise with the Emperor Sertorius, that they lack
victual, and waxed so hongrie after, that all kind of beasts were slain,
they were enforced to eat their own wives and children. It was seen in
Jerusalem, when that it was destroyed by Vespasian the Emperor of Rome,
Vespasian.
by the Roman soldiers, how that the mothers were compelled to eat their
own children, Cap. 57.
for very honger, for their small and tender bones, were left as a show of
their misery. Plini in his eighte of natural histories, that when Hanibal
laid siege unto the City Casilinum, the Romans soldiers were in such
honger, that one Mouse was sold for two hundred pieces of silver, and he that
sold the Mouse died him self for honger. Athens.
The Athenians likewise were brought unto such honger by Silla, which
afterward was Dictatour in Rome, that one Bushel of wheat was sold emongst
the soldiers for a thousand Drachmis, the common soldiers being poor, for
want of money one way, and sore plagued with honger an other way were
compelled to eat the green grass of the fields, about the City of Athens,
and to gather the Moss of the walls of the City and eat it. This City of
Athens was often times brought unto that misery, as by king Demetrius, king
Philippe, and his son Alexander the great: So much was famine feared
emo^gest the aunciente Greeks, that they used in the time of aboundancie, to
scourge famine with rods out of their houses saying: For as famen intro
diuitias, away penury, come in ple^tie. Alexander.
We read in Q. Curtius, that Alexander was driven by honger to eat his
Cameller, Elephantes, and other huge beasts that carried the trains for the
wars. Such honger and famine did happen among the Lacedaemonians, that the
Citizeins of Sparta were so hongrie, that they eat the very Serpents that
were dead a long while, which mutitude of Serpents, which before were
drouned, did presage this great calamity to come, of whom the people of
Sparta, though they were dead a long time, yet most hongerly fed them self,
and mitigated the rage of famine. Doda king of Siria besieging a great famous
city in Iewrie called Iora, Doda.
where the miserable mothers were of mere honger, enforced to feed of the
bowels of their own children. Not much unlike unto that horrible and cruel
famine, in the country of Apulea, being driven of the Frenchemen, then their
enemies in wars, that the soldiers were compelled to take the skins from
their Bucklardes, and to warm and boil the hard horns, and to eat them. To
entreat of the wou^derfull calamities, misery, and plague that happened
through honger, it were to much the charge thereof, too many aucthorities are
manifest in this behalf. Antonius whom Augustus Caesar could never vanquishe
with force of arms, he was driven to yield in Etruria, Perusia.
in a City called Perusia, by honger and famine. Wherefore that noble Athenian
Nicias, always thought the easieste way, the spidiest cause of yeldyng unto
the enemies was Famine, which he showed at Melos, a City of Thessalie,
whereby he made the Citizeins to yield by honger. O ragyng force of Famine, O
terrible misery of man, which compel the parents to eat the children, the
children to kill their pare^tes: what beast was spared ever, when this
happened. The people named Hymmi through ho^ger were constrained to eat their
own Dogs, Hymmi.
as the Macedonians sometime fed them selves with Camelles, Elephantes,
Horses, and such like. What herb was unsought? what root was not found, to
feed this cruel monster. The fields of Piceni.
As Sabellicus does witness, of dearthe that chanced in his time, that in
some parts of the country of Flaminia, and about the fields called Piceni
the common people did live by grass and herbs, and by such like that
proceeded from the earth. This was the world ever plagued with Famine, as with
that monster that spoileth and deuoureth it self, as we read of diverse that did
eat their own arms and flesh. Again in the sacred scripture, diverse
examples we have of the like sent from God, to plague man. But because honger
one way is most excellent, if meat may be had, so hounger an other is
most terrible, if the same fail. Stratonicus.
Therefore Stratonicus never went to bed without a Cup of drink by him, not
for that he thrusted, when he went unto bed, but lest he thursted in the
bed, which should compel to do some injury with one or other, Alphonsus.
for that he wanted drink. So did Alpho^sus king of Arragon, when he saw the
poor country man, greedily feedyng on Grapes said, for that he could not be
hongrie. O would the gods had framed me to be such one as this is. So that
hounger is good unto those that want the same. Gnefactus.
For Gnefactus king of Egypt, having his men of arms in the deserts of
Arabia wantyng victuales waxed so hongrie, that the poor fare and simple
cheer, that he got amongst the country men, was so acceptable unto him, that
he caused a table for a monumente of the same, in the temple of Jupiter in
Thebes. Of diverse famine we read in scripture, that Abraham fled from the
land of Chananea unto Egypt: and Isaac driven by famine unto Abimelech king of
the Palestines, and all the soonnes of Jacob, were enforced to go to king
Pharao, where their own brother Joseph ruled as chilef officer. Famine is
appoincted for a just scourge to sin* as appears by the filthy sin of
king David, toward Urias wife, he had to choose either plague, famine, or
wars, which are the instruments to punish offenders.

Of wariness.
WE leave Apollo in Delphos, and Jupiter in Boetia, with their wise answers
and Oracles, we will not speak of Socrates, Solon, and thousands such as
were coumpted and known wise* and discrete amongst Greeks and Gentiles, we
will only entreat of those worldly and natural wisdom, which by their
prudent policy, and wary practices have aduau^ced their fame thereby, as well
in discoursyng and vanquishyng their enemies, as also by inuentyng such
sleightes and snares, for the obtainyng of the same, as their wits thereby
were worthily commended, as Hanibal perceiuyng the courage, and strength of
the Romans, used this witty feate: gathered a great number of Serpents,
and put them in huge vessels, and made them to be brought unto the field
amongst his soldiers, commaundyng the Capitaines and chief officers, to
throw the same unto the force of the enemies, who being thereby astonished,
forsook and fled the field, as men bereste of wits* spoiled of sense,
and almost in despair of them selves, thinkyng Deuels, and not men to be
the soldiers of Hanibal. Hanibals sleightes.
Of the like wisdom was king Cirus, who being in his tents, Cirus craft.
and ready to pitche field the next day with the Messagetes, commaundyng his
soldiers to be in a readiness, that night to flee their tents, leaving
behind their victuales and substance, that the enemies being busy of the
spoil, and giuyng open to banquettyng, and Carousyng of wine, he with all his
army might unawares return, and findyng the Messagets more greedy of the
spoil, then ready for their enemies destroy and kille them, as it came to
pass. So that in wars, says Salust, wit does as much good as
strength, policy better accepted then power, and as Virgill says, so that
victory be gotten, way not whether it be through courage, or through
policy. For Sertorius that worthy Emperor and captain of Rome, was wont by
lies and letters, by dreams and outward religion, by all means possible,
fainyng and inuentyng thousand ways, to stir his soldiers to courage. The
inventions of wit is much, and so diverse, that to much it were to repeat.
Sicyonius.
How Sicionius deceived Xerxes with all his soldiers through policy. How
Pisistratus moved the Athenians, Pysistratus.
to revenge his false wrongs upon the chief officers of Athens. How Darius
after Cambises death became king of Perse, Darius.
by means of a horse, and such like. But lettyng pass infinite nombers of
such, and to declare what Nature wroughte in selie and simple beasts, in
^iyng fowls, and in the very fish swimmyng in the water. The lion.
The lio^ by nature is taught being very sick, to find out an Ape, which
by outward sportes and pastyme, does heal his great sever and grief. The
Elephant.
The huge Elephante is so subtle when he is like to die, will seek by all
means the cameleon bow, which he so estemeth, that his sickness forsakes him
straight. The Panther.
The Pa^ther knows by nature his ready salve for this sore, for feelyng him
self not well, he straight seeks out the dounge of man, which by the sent
thereof, he heals him self. The stircken Heart feedyng on high Mountains,
The Heart.
has that consideration, at what time he is shot through with any darte or
arrowe, by feedyng of an herb called Dictamum, their blood stanche, and their
wounds heal. The Bear.
And the Bear is so crafty, that by the same he is taught being sick, to
lick and eat up little Antes for her appoincted Physic. Even so fliyng
fowls do know their appoincted salve, for their sores by nature taught. The
Raven. The Ducke. The Dove: The Swallow.
The Raven, the Ducke, the Swallowe swift, yea, the sely Mice do before hand
presage their ruinous state by Nature, and know well the decaie of any house,
barn, or place where they be, and will change hospitality before the time,
if necessity happen upon them. The little Antes full of toil and travail, to
gather in the Summer, to serve them in the Winter. These with diverse others,
Plini in his.8. book. Chapter.27. and Aristotle in his book De natura
animaliu^, do make mention. W readee in Aelianus diverse worthy histories of
the like, The Cranes of Cicilia.
but specially of the Cranes of Sicilia, which when they be about to take
their slightes from Sicilia, to fly over mount Caucasus, they are so crafty,
and subtle by nature, that they bear in their mouths certain stones, to
stop their cry and noise, which Craines most commonly use in flight, lest
by hearing of their voice and cry, the Egles of Caucasus should destroy them.
The Goats of Crete, when they be shot through with darts and arrows, are
of the^ self moved to feed on a certain herb, which straight stencheth the
blood, heals the wound, and expels out the venime of the wound. There is
such craft and subtlety in a little Frogge of Nilus, that when the huge and
monstrous Troute comes toward him, to destroy him, the Frogge by and by out
of hand* bears a long reed ouerth warte his mouth, and so marches
forward toward this great champion, that by no mean he can destroy him, for
that the reed is longer, then his mouth can swalowe the same, and so the
little Frogge escapes the terror of this great beast. What a slight has a
fish called Polipos, which being desirous to feed on any fish, he goes and
hides him self under some shrubbe, or rock, or any other means, whereby he
seems to be, as though he were a tile or a stone, until the fish come to
that place, then he leapeth upon him, and kills him. So that there is no
beast, no foul, no fish, but has as it were a certain privilege by nature
to defend him self and to foil his foe, and that craftily by nature taught.
There is again a kind of knowledge in beast to know their friends, and to love
them, and to fear their enemies, and to avoid them. As the Serpents in
Terinthia, the Scorpio^s in Arcadia, and the Snakes of Siria, which Plini
affirms, that they will not hurt their country men, and known friends,
though they found the^ him a sleep, as diverse and sondrie times, histories
make mention thereof. Strange therefore is the work of nature, which
mightily displaieth her self in all living creatures, and for the proof
thereof, I will note one history written of Quintilia^ in his 14. book of
histories, that in Achaia there was a city named Patra, in the which a
certain young man bought a little dragon, which with great care and diligence,
he nourished until it waxed big liyng in one chamber in the night time, and
plaiyng all the day time. At length the Magistrates of the City fearyng, that
some hurt should be done by this Dragon, considering the fierce and cruel
nature of them, did let him to go unto the wilderness, where diverse other
dragons were. And there being a long time, this young man that brought up this
Dragon with diverse of his fellows passing by, where this dragon was, certain
thieues assailed them, and he by his voice was known by this Dragon, which as
he was heard, came out of the woddes, and seeing him with diverse of his fellows
like to be murdered, flewe unto the very faces of the thieues, and so strongly
fought with them, that some the Dragon flue, some sore hurt, and some
constrained to fly, saved him and his fellows, in recompense of his former
courtesy. Surely I judge it a better benefit bestowed upon such a Dragon,
then upon some ungrateful persons that be in the world.

Of reuengemente.
THe best way to revenge any injury offered, is to suffer quietly the same,
and to show virtue toward vice, goodness toward evil, honesty toward
scurrilitie, which is the only poison unto the enemy, as for an example.
Laertius does manifest the same by comparisons of things: Who is he that
sees his enemies fields green, his pastures well grassed, his house
furnished, and all things in comely order, but he is a grieved therewith, how
much more said he, when the envious sees his foe adorned with all virtuous,
compassed with all patience, yea, prosperyng in all goodness, he is therewith
molested. And in that place of his sixte book, he recites a worthy history,
and a noble example, of due reuengement by Diogines the Cinicke Philosopher,
who by chance came, where diverse young men were at banquet, making merry, his
head being balde, by reason of age, he was so flouted and scoft of most part
of the company, that with stripes and strokes, they threw him out of the
house: the poor old Philosopher revenged his wrong in this wise He took a
piece of white Chalk, and wrote the names of all those that so used him upon
his cloak, and so opened his cloak, that all men might read their names, and
know how wickedly they had used him, and what scoffs and floutes he had
suffered of those persons, whose names were to be read upon his cloak, and so
brought them in such blame with all men, that they wished in heart that they
never had seen Diogenes, that made all the world to se their folly, that
afterward they were noted as ridiculous persons, not worthy of honest
company, and so were they excluded from the good men, banished from civil
men, and quite forsaken of all honest and virtuous men. Agesilaus.
Agesilaus king of the Lacedaemonians, when he had heard of certain foes of
his, that always spoke ill of his person, and of his state he after this sort
revenged him: he chused and elected them, chief capitanes over his men of
arms, and committed all the charge of his hooste unto his enemies, whereby he
made his foes to become his friends, yea, his servants and slaves, to do
what he would command them. For so Demosthenes did when he was provoked, and
injuriously handled of him, that was in a banquet disposed to fall out, and
fight with him, no said Demosthenes, I will never take that in hand, which
the victor thereof, thereby sustaineth shame. O worthy sentence, and most
aptly aplied unto a wiseman. We read in Brusonius of Dion of Alexandria: Dion.
who with silence revenged more his foes, then with words, for being provoked
unto anger by a vilaine, and abiecte, which followed him through the streate,
chidyng and threatenyng him, answered not one word, but bade him good night,
when he came unto the very door of Dion, which when the enemy saw, he
would not be moved unto anger, to do him hurt, whereby he might do the
like unto him again, he went unto the next tree and hanged him self. Thus did
Socrates, Socrates.
being blamed of his friend for his silence, in that he was injuriously handled
of his foe, answered and said, that his enemies saying could not damage him,
since he was not that manne, that the words did importe him to be, and being
stricken and spurned by the same man, Socrates,
Socrates was cou^sailed to call the same unto the Law before the Judges, unto
the which he answered: which of you if an Ass strike him, will call that
Ass before any judges, since he is no better that thus uses me For by this am
I known to be Socrates, and he known to be an Ass. The greatest reuengemente
unto a fool, is to let every man know his folly: and the greateste hurt
unto a wiseman, is to revenge folly, for it was all the reuengemente of
Socrates, when any man spoke ill of him, to say thus, he never was taught to
speak well. So courteous was that Fabius Maximus, that when he had heard that
one of his chief soldiers, was about to betraie him unto his enemies, he
called the party before him, not making him privy what he knew of him, but
demaundyng of Marsius what he wa^ted, and willing him to ask any thing he
should have, and so made him chief capitain of the army: By this means he
became most true unto Fabius, being before most false. This was far from
such reuengementes, Alexander.
as Alexander the Great did, who after he had subdued diverse Kingdoms and
Countries, he went unto the Temple of Ammon, to know by the Oracles of
Jupiter, whether yet any were a live that slew his father king Philip, whereby
he might show more tyranny, Brutus.
and practice murder further. This was far from M. Brutus rage, which being
not content to usurp Caesar, yea, to kill him in the Senate house: but also
when power failed, when soldiers decayed, and he almost vanquished, made his
prayers unto Jupiter, and unto the host of heaven, to plage Caesar and his
posterities. This I say, was far from Liuius Salinator, who being warned of
Fabius Maximus, not to revenge malice upon Hasdruball, before he knew, the
state of the matter, the power of the field, and the end of the victory,
where it should happen: But he more rashely to revenge, then wise in sufferyng,
said that either out of hand kill, or be killed. Antheus.
And in this place I will recite 3. or 4. histories fit for this purpose.
With Antheus a noble gentleman of Halicarnassus, being in pledge left with
Phobius, chief Ruler then of Millesia, fell Phobius wife in love, vsyng all
means possible to allure Antheus in love with her. But he partly for fear,
and partly for love of Phobius her housebande, Cleoboea.
would in no wise consent to any filthye desire, of this Cleoboea Phobius
wife, which she took in so evil part, that she began mortally to hate
him, inuentyng what way best she might revenge his cruel incurtesie, in
refusyng her love. She fained on a time, that she had quite forgotten her old
love toward him, and thanked Antheus very much for the love, and great
zeal that he bare unto her housbande Phobius, in not consentyng to her folly
then, when she was in love with him. Thus talkyng with him, Cleoboea brought
her old lover Antheus over a Well, where for that purpose only, she threw a
tame Partriche, desiryng him to aid her to have her Partriche out of the
Well, the young gentleman misdoubtyng her in nothing, as one willing to
pleasure his friend and old lover, went down into the Well to have the
Partriche out, but she revenged her old love, and requited his service then
after this sort: She threw a great stone after him, and there killed him,
and straight for sorrow, calling to mind the old amity, and hidden love
between them, hanged her self. The reuengement yt Cleonimus, Cleonimus.
that noble and famous Lacedemonian, who having his own wife in such
admiration, of impacient love, that he was as much hated of her, as she of him
was honored and esteemed, for she loved only king Acrotatus son so dear,
that her housebande Cleonimus understanding the same, went to Epire to king
Pirrhus, perswadyng him earnestly to come to Peloponesus, and to move wars
against king Acrotatus, whereby he might revenge the spite done of his wife, in
killyng him whom she loved best, a great reuengemente as he thought unto her,
then to revenge upon her own person, to spoil him whom she love better then
her self. Valerius Torquatus.
Valerius Torquatus, for that he might have Tuscus daughter in marriage moved
wars out of hand, and revenged the same with blood. Progne.
For what cause did Progne king Pandions daughter of Athens kill her own son
Itis, and gave him to be eaten unto his father, and her housebande king Tereus
of Thrace, nothing but to revenge her sister Philomela, whom her housebande
defloured her. Why did Nero that cruel Emperor kille Seneca his master, Nero.
and teacher in all his youth: for nothing but to revenge old stripes, which
he received at his master, being a boie. For what purpose did Cateline Silla,
Damasippus Marius and other, take quarelles to plage Rome, to punish all
Italy, to destroy the country, for nothing, but for that they could not
abide one above an other. Darius.
Darius after that he had taken the City of Babylon, he revenged his old
malice after this sort, as Herodot in his third book affirms: He made three
thousand of the best within the City be hanged. Attila.
Attila King of Pannonia slew a leven thousand virgins, at the besiegyng of
Colonia. So diverse were reuengeme^t amongst men, Xerxes. Herodotus^ lib. 7.
so cruel, yea so foolish that Xerxes and Cirus, two great kings of
Persia, that when the water of Hellespont molested Xerxes, and troubled his
soldiers, he forth with commanded that the sea Helespont should have three
hundred stripes: and willed three hundred pair of Featters to be thrown unto
Hellespont, to bind the sea. Even so did Cirus for that the river Gindes, did
droune one of Cirus best geldynges, he made his soldiers to divide the river
unto a hundred, and four score small parts, to revenge Gindes rage toward
Cirus, thinkyng that by breaking of the great rage of so great a stream,
that he well and worthily requited the injuries of Gindes. These are cruel
reuengers, too many are of these, in so much that women revenge their malice
after this sort. As Tomiris Queen of Scithia, Tomiris.
who to revenge her soonne Mergabites death slew king Cirus, and two
thousands of his soldiers: Too great a slaughter for one man's death, and
not yet satisfied, until she bathed Cirus head in a great vessel full of
bloudde. Beronice. Poll. a.
This Beronice Pollia, and diverse cruel women beside could do, the one is
dooen with anger and sin, the other is dooen with virtue, and aduisemente.
For princes must use aduisement in revenging, and must use wisdom in
sufferaunce. For as Frederick the Emperor, was often wont to say, that
Princes that revenge hastily, and specially wrongfully, are like fair marks
for good archers to shoot at High towers and lofty buildings, are sooner
fiered with lightenynges, then low houses, and small cottages. Tiberius.
For Tiberius Caesar Emperor of Rome, being in the Senate house, to punish
those evils, and to revenge those harms, that were by some of the city
threatened toward his estate. God forbid said he, that Tiberius should have
so much idle time, to hear evil spoken, much less to revenge evil done.
Antigonus king of Macedonia, Antigonus.
besiegyng a Castle in Greece, wherein a number of bold Greeks used for their
pastyme and sport, to scoff this king, knowing the situation of the Castle to
be in such a place, that might not be subdued. They therefore laughyng him
to scorn, as well for his enterprice therein* as also for his slender person,
and crooked nose, which king Antigonus had said, he would revenge all their
doings, with sufferaunce, and hoped thereby to moleste the enemies double.
Diverse Heathen princes were acquainted with this reuengement: as Lisander,
Agesilaus, and others, for unto God only belongs vengeance. I will not
speak here of such reuengyng, of Princes, of Countries, of friends that al
men know. But of rare reuengement, Socrates.
which Philosophy taught unto Socrates, toward Xantippe, who being at
supper, having a strange geste named Enthidemum, his wife Xantippe began to
take her housbande up, with tauntyng and opprobrious words, which because he
would not auswere, and be moved by her chidinges, she ouerthrewe the table with
all the meat and the Cups, which when Enthidemum saw, he was amazed at the
ragyng of Xantippe, beholding Socrates in the face, to see how he thought of
the matter, but Socrates understanding that his geste did marvel at his wife,
said: have not you sometime at home a Hen, that will after long clockyng
with a sudden flight, throw down your cups with her wyng, wherewith
Enthidemum was fully satisfied, with the wise answer of Socrates, in
reuengyng so great a fault. Phocio^.
Phocion a learned man of Athens, was wont to say, that he had rather suffer
injury wrongfully, then to revenge injury sometime rightfully. This man
Phocion, by whom Athens long flourished, at what time he was put to death
most wrongfully of the Athenians, even a little before he should die, being
demanded whether he would command any thing unto his soonne, standing
thereby to see his father end: Spoke unto his son after this sort: My son
said he, this I charge and require thee, and moreover beseech, that thou will
never revenge the wrongful death of thy father Phocion upon the Athenians.
Solon a noble learned Athenian, Solon.
was wont to revenge his wrongs with these words. If the fissherman suffer
the salt water of the Sea, to sprinkle upon his face, and upon his clothes, to
weate him for to take Fish, how much more ought Solon suffer to speak, to
win the^ to be friends. Surely these three Philosophers, deserve more praise
and commendation I mean Socrates, Phocion, and Solon for the reuengyng of the
evil with goodness and virtue: Then ever Alexander the Great, or Julius
Caesar, or Theseus, which revenged evil with evil. Wherefore Chilon the
Lacedemonian, being one of the officers called Ephori, in the City of Sparta,
his brother demaundyng why he might not be likewise one of the five Ephori, as
well as his brother: said unto his brother, because I can suffer wrong, and
thou canste not. Therefore princes ought not to do wrong nor yet revenge wrong
with wrong, but with paciente sufferaunce and goodness, and doing good for
evil, they shall make foes to become friends, evil men to become good, by
preuentyng evil with lenity and gentleness. It behooves not a wiseman to
revenge injuries, neither does it become a prince to requite evil with the
like, but overcome rather the evil with good. Therefore was it truly spoken
of the wiseman Sapit qui sustinet, he that can suffer is very wise.

Of Theft and Sacrilege.
AFTER that greedy desire unto wealth had possessed a place in man's heart, and
after that the world was altered from a wealth in common, unto a private
wealth, every man with study and industry to augment his own, with the
spoil of others. And because Princes began one to suppress an other, to
spoil and destroy either others dominion, mouyng first noble men to imitate
them in stealyng and taking away perforce others wealth, thought it be not an
apt epitheton for princes to be called thieves, and spoylers, yet truly by
Princes it began, by nobles imitated, and by all the world at length practiced,
that some became Pirates upon the Seas, some Sacriledges of Temples, and some
graunde Thieves of countries and kingdoms. For after the deluge of Noah, there
was neither theft or sacrilege known almost 300. years until Ninus the
third, Ninus.
king of the Assirians who first began to play the Theif in Asia, Dionisius.
Dionisius, king of Sicilia and tyrant of all the world, the greatest robber
that ever reigned upon earth, being not satisfied with spoil and theft upon
Lands and Seas, but also became a Sacrilege in the Temples of the gods, which
he so neglected, that after he robbed the Temple of Iuppiter in Olimpia, he
passed forth unto Locris to spoil the Temple of Proserpina, and from thens
unto Epidaurus to steal the golden beard of Aesculapius. This Tyraunte king
would not suffice him self, until he worthily had merited the name of a thief a
pirate, Xerxes.
and a sacrilege. Xerxes spared not emongeste others wicked spoils, to send
four thousand of his soldiers to Delphos, to rob the Temple of Apollo,
Spartacus a great prince, Spartia*
and a maintainer of thieues, gathered a whole army of fugitive persons,
vagaboundes, thieues, and robbers, and marched toward Rome, with wars either
to conquer Rome, or to be conquered by Rome, but there was he, and all roges
vanquished by Pu. Crassus. The City of Rome was often in peril by Thieues and
robbers, as by Silla Catilin, and Marius famous spoilers of Italy. And as
Cercion did rob and spoil after the Country of Athe^s, so Tittigias in
Arcadia was renoumed. For theft I might in this place, speak of the robbery of
the Emperor Nero, Nero. Heliogabal^^. Caligula.
of the spoil and waste of that beastly Emperor Heliogabalus, and of the
sacrilege and theft of Caligula. These three Emperors stale alwais, spoiled,
and took from Rome, more then ever they gave to Rome. Marcellinus writes,
that there was sometime a King of the Parthians named Arsaces, Arsaces.
which in the beginning of his reign, was then named the master of thieves, a
teacher and a schoolemaister unto all robbers and spoilers, which afterward he
subdued Seleucus Alexanders successoure, he became famous and renoumed in
marshall feats, and civil policy. Herodotus likewise does report of one
Amazis, Amasis.
a King of Egipte, who when at any time money wanted, he was wont to spoil,
waste, and take a way all that ever he might, either by stealthe, or force.
Thus the names of Princes first was corrupted, that the Poets judged well and
worthily, Mercury to be the God of Thieves And for the antiquity of theft,
it is thought that Prometheus Duealions Father, as Poets do faigue, by the
aid of Minerua, stool first fire from Phoebus, for the which fact, was
punished in mount Caucasus, after this sort he was bound fast, by all the
Gods and an Egle appoincted to eat up his heart, and to hale his puddynges a
long, in furtheraunce and memory of his theft. Hercules and Jason, two of the
most famous princes that ever Greece fostered, went unto Cholcus, to steal the
golden Fleece. Theseus and Perithus went unto the kingdom of Pluto, to steal
Proserpina away. But I will not speak of such Theft, for too many examples
should be read in that behalf. If I might use such scope of writing, but I
will briefly pass over, and speak only of aunciente, and famous Thieves,
which for that the number is infinite, I (omittyng tediousness) speak of
few for a proof of my matter. There was dwelling in a rock nigh Athens* a
famous Thief named Sciron, which was wont to throw headlong, strangers
that were gestes, over a rock unto the sea, and after that he had continewed a
long time in spoilyng, and murtheryng of men that passed by, in taking their
gooddes, and lives away, he was in the same sort of Theseus put to death on
the rocks unto the Seas, as he was wont to do with others. Cacus whom Virgil
makes mention, the son of Vulcanus was so crafty a thief, that having a
den in mount Auentine, he used to draw any thing bacwarde by the tail, unto
his cave where he spoiled it, whether it were manne or beast, there should he
be brought, Cacus.
by sleight of Cacus to be destroyed, until he attempted to spoil Hercules
by stealthe, which after long wrastlyng in his den, Hercules with his clubbe
slew him. Scinius.
The famous Thief Scinius used such feats, and theftes about Corinth, that
he would bind any passer by or strangers unto trees, and there would hew
them unto small gobbettes for their money and substance. These three last
renoumed Thieves, are much mentioned of writers, for even as Capiton kept him
self fifty years in a den, as a common robber to steal and to spoil, so
did they consume their time severally, and in sondery countries, as graund
Thieves. The Argiues were men most noted infamous, for this fault, in so
much that a Proverb grew of the Argiues, Argiuifures, that is, Argiues are
Thieves. With the Persians there were certain thieves called Cardaces,
permitted without punishment, to steal and to rob. The old Germans and
ancient Egiptians might sometime by law, and liberty of their country
likewise steal. Lycurgus made laws in Sparta, emo^gest the Lacedemonia^s,
that he which did steal without reprehentions, or taking with the theft,
should be free, and he which could not artificially steal, and attempt
Theft being taken, should be punished, in so much Brusonius in his second
book, Brusonius.
does entreat of a young man, that stole a young Fox, the owner thereof
followyng after, demaundyng whether the young man, saw any where a little
Fox, he denied, chidyng the Fox under his cloak, but the Fox a subtle
beast, willing to show him self unto his master, did bite and scratche the
yongman so sore, that his puddynges gusshed out of his side, because he
suffered him self so to die, by reason that he would not manifest his theft.
The yongman then deniyng, answered and said: I had rather die, then to be
taken with theft. Wherefore Theophrastus a noble Philosopher, Theophrastus.
having the examinyng of a subtle Thief, demanded whether he could blusshe
or no, to the which the thief answered that he could not, for he need not
to blushe, in a true matter: Therefore says Theophrastus, thou art the liker
to be a Thief, for truth always bears before a shamefaste, and a blushfull
countenance. Wherefore the wise Cato the Senior was wont to say, that
yongmen that waxed redd, were better to be trusted, then those that would wax
pale, for the one signifies shamfastnes, and the other deceit. For Pithias
Aristotle's daughter being demanded, what color was best in man or woman, she
answered, that color that shamefastnes brings, which is a bluffull
countenance. But to speak of Pirates, Sextus Pompeius the son of Pompeius
the G^eate, kept under him diverse and sondery Pirates, about the borders of
Italy, and Cicilia, to rob and spoil upon the Seas, unto his great infamy
and reproach, being the soonne of so famous a Roman, whom Rome a long time
so esteemed, that Caesar skante might have the like. To write of King Pirrhus,
Pirrhus. Verres.
and Caius Verres, whom Cicero for his sondery theftes and spoil, and by
diverse sacrileges by Verres committed, compared unto Dionisius the aforesaid
tiraunt, it were but superfluous. To speak of infinite Pirates, and diverse
Sacrileges, it were to none effect for that it is a common practice in all
countries. Therefore as Diogines the Philosopher said, when he saw a poor
manne lead between the Magistrates, to the place of execution, behold said
he, a little Thief between a great number of Thieves. God grant that it may
truly be spoken of diverse magistrates in sundry places.

Of lust.
THE spoil and slaughter of lust, did always far pass all other vices, it
has suppressed Castles and countries, it has vanquished Kings and Kesars,
ouerthrown the po^pe of Asia, Africa and Europe, and almost depopulated the
whole world. This vice of all vices is to be abhorred and detested, for there
is no vice but it is addicted of it self to apply those, which it does best
fancy, as pride chiefly has her seat appoincted in puisant Princes and Noble
men. Coueteousnes with old men that be magistrates and officers. Envy with
men of sciences and faculties. Usurie with Citizens, Symonie with Bishops and
Priests, Hypocrisy with religious men, Deceit with Merchants, but lust
common unto all men, as well to the subject as to the Prince, to the learned as
to the ignorant, t^ the wise as to the foolish. David.
For David and his son Salamon, Solomon.
unto whom God gave singularity of wisdom, dexterity of wit to govern the

Israel. Aristotle. Socrates.
Aristotle and Socrates in spit of their Philosophy and great knowledge, then

There is a history worthy to be noted of Princes in Iustine that will not

Of Ielowsie.
A Question refused of all the Gods to be answered whether man or woman is
more ielowsie, for as the poets feign, there sprung a contention between
Jupiter and Juno, concerning Lechery and Ielowzy, and having no equal judge to
speak of this matter, it was posted, after great controversy unto one
Tiresias an ancient and learned Poet sometime in Thebes, The history of
Tiresias,
which Tiresias on a certain time metyng two Snakes accompanyng together,
and according unto kind engenderyng together, having a white rod in his
hand, departed them with sharp strokes. Wherewith Juno being moved with
anger, transformed this poor Poet Tiresias, from a man to be a woman, and
being in the shape of a woman seven years, was reduced by Jupiter unto his
first form. This Tiresias was thought most meet of Jupiter and Juno, by the
consent of all the Gods, for that he had been a woman seven years, and now
a man again to Judge of this question. And being called unto the Bar to give
his Uerdite, Juno*
he preferred Juno for Ielowsie, whereby Juno waxed angry, and made him
blind, and Jupiter to recompense his truth, made him a Prophet. And then he
proved that when Jupiter fell in love with Iolla, Juno being suspicious and
full of Ielowsie, caused one named Argos with an hundred eyes to watch
Jupiter, which for all his eyes was deceived, Juno thereby was so furious and so
angry with Argos, that she translated his hundred eyes unto a Pecockes tail,
and transformed Iola unto a white Cow. There is no such rage nor anger in
Ielowsie, as there is wilines and craft in love, so that the straight pinning
and keeping of Danaes king Acrisius daughter in Towers and Castles, cold never
keep her from valiant Perseus, neither the hundred eyes of Argos might spy the
craft of Jupiter unto Iola. We read of a womanne named Procris, Procris.
who was in such Ielowsie of her housbande which was called Cephalus, and
having him in suspicion for his often going a huntyng, on a certain time she
followed him privily unto the woddes, thinkyng there to find her housbande at
his pray, and hiding her self in a thick bush, to see the end of things,
her housband passing by the bush, perceiuyng somthyng there, to steer, thinkyng
it had been some wild beast, thrust his wife unto the heart with his darte, and
thus Procris was slain of her own housbande, for her importunate Ielowsie. The
like happened unto Aemilius wife, which for her suspicious mind and ragyng
ielowsie, never quiet, but busy always to find some fault in her housband,
following him every where, and watchyng still in every secrette seat and
spiyng in privy places, thinking to find him with the manner, until she spead
of the like chance as Procris did, she could never rest. Cyampus wife named
Leuconoa, was devoured of dogs in stead of a wild beast, hidyng them self in
the woddes, to follow and mark her housbandes viage, Ielowsie this moved her,
that she could no otherwise. A strange kind of sickness, that so infecteth the
mind, that vexes the spirits and molesteth the heart, that the head is full
of invention, the mind full of thought, and the heart full of reuengement. So
Ielows was Phanius that invented this in his head, Phanius*
and thought this in his mind, that the doors being shut, the windows
cloase, all privy and secret places prevented, every where as he thought
stopte, his wife could not deceive him, never thought that love could pearce
Tile stones to come unto his wife, but he was deceived, for the lurkyng dens
of love, the Lion caves of fancies, the secret search of affection have more
privy paths whereby that Cupide may come to his mother Venus, then
Labiranthus had chambers for Minotaurus, king Acrisius thought he was so sure
of his daughter Danaes, when that she was close bulwarkt with a great castle,
Juno thought to prevent Jupiter by the hundred eyes of Argos, Phanius thought
that his wife was sure when the doors were shut and the windows cloase. But
since the ielowsie of Juno might not prevent it, nether the eyes of Argos spy
it, neither the straight pinning of Danaes avoid it, neither the narow stopping
of Phanius defend it. I must needs commend one called Cippius, Cippius
that would oftentimes take upon him to sleep when he did wake, and he would
be ignorant though he knew it, I wish wise men to sleep with Cippus, and
to say with Cicero, Non omnibus dormio, I sleep not unto all men, and to be
ignorant though they know things. And likewise I wish wise women to imitat
Aemilia ye wife of noble Scipio, Aemilia.
who although she knew things evident, by her housbande Scipio, made as much
of his Paramour, as she made of her housband, and al for Scipios sake. For they
say ielowsie proceadeth from love, and love from God, but I say it comes
from hatered, and hatered comes from the Devil. And because we read in the
sacred scripture, that Abraham was in ielowsie of his wife Sara, saying, thus
unto his wife, I know that thou art fair, and that they will kill me to have
thy love, the manners of the Parthia^s were to keep their wives in pruie places
of their houses, over whom they were so jealous that their wives might not go
abroad but with covered faces. The Perseans were so suspicious of their wives,
that they had no liberty to go in sight, and they durst not go a foot but in
Wagons covered over lest they should see or be seen. The Thracians with such
care and study keep their wives, that as Herodotus affirms, they trust no man with them in company but their parents. The old and ancient Romans
in times past kept their wives so straight, that their wives as Valerius
Maximus says, did after kill, poison or with some snare or other destroy
their housbandes diverse times, and by a young manne of the City of Rome all
things being discloased, there was a hundred three score and ten that so
killed and destroyed their housbandes, for that their housbandes were so ielous
over them, but because it is a common disesse in all places, I need not
further to write, wishyng my friend never to be encombred therewith but rather
with silence to pass it with Cippius, and so he shall find ease thereby.

Of Idleness.
AS nothing can be more difficult unto a willing mind, so is any thing a
burden unto the Idle member. For as labor and exercise of body in one manne,
industry and diligence of mind in an other man, are sure fortes, and strong
Bulwarckes of Countries, so Idleness and negligence, Alexander the Great,
the cause of all evil. We read that Alexander the Great, least he should
be acquainted with Idleness at any time, even in the night time used this feate,
to hold a silver balle when he went unto bed in his hand, Marcellini lib.
xvi.
having a silver basen upon the ground, straight under his stretched arm,
that when the batle should fall, he being fast a sleep, the shrill sound
thereof should wake him, and make him mindful of his enemies: so fearful was
this noble prince of Idleness, that to shake of sleep and slothfulness, he
studied and travailed, how he might avoid it. For in two things Alexander
the Great, being called the son of Jupiter, and fully persuaded with him
self, that he was of lineage of the Gods, yet in sleep and venerie, where to he
was much subject, he knew him self to be a man, wherefore he oftentimes
wrasteled with Nature in that behalf. In the self same place of Marcellinus it
is read, Caesar.
that Julius Caesar the great and most renowned Emperor, that ever reigned
in Rome, to have followed this order, and to have practiced this policy, least
he should be idle at any time, First, when this Emperor went to bed, he to
suffice nature, slepte a certain time appoincted. Secondarely, he would be
occupied in the affairs of his country. Thirdly, to travail in his private
study. Thus lest he should be idle, naie rather lest he should lose any
time, he divided every night in three parts, even as you heard first unto
nature, secondly to his country, thirdly, about his own business. That mighty
prince Philip of Macedon, Lib. vi. Capi. 8.
as we read in Brusonius, was of such care and diligence, when his soldiers
slept, he always watched. Again, he never slept before his friend. Antipater
would watch. Philippe. Antipater.
That between king Philip and Antipater, diligence was as much honored and
embraced, as slothfulnes was feared and hated. Epaminondas.
Epaminondas that renoumed prince of Thebes, being studious and careful to
profit his country, so hated slothful idleness, that findyng one of his
capitaines in the camp, in the day time slepyng, slew him straight with his
own hand, and being reprehended of his nobles and counsel, for that cruel
fact: he answered the^ in few words, I left him as I found him, comparyng
idle and drousie men, unto dead men, for men are borne to trauaill and watch,
and not to pleasure and sleep. How did Scipio Afric overthrow the tents of
king Siphax? Scipio. Siphax.
How vanquished he his host of soldiers, slew his army and how he has taken
king Siphax captive him self. Liuius says, that the diligence of Scipio, and
the slothfulnes of Siphax being a sleep, when he should had been waken,
Demosthenes.
was the cause thereof. Had Demosthenes loved idleness, he had never been able to
prevent, that famous prince Philip king of Macedon, he was so careful and so
diligent about the state of Athens, that that worthy captain and great
conqueror Philip was wont to say, that he doubted more the diligence of
Demosthenes, then he feared all the force of Athens. Had Cicero slepte, during
the conspiracy of Catilin, he had never been able worthily to speak of him
self. O happy Rome, that ever I was elected Consul, whose studious trauaill,
saved oftentimes Rome from diverse enemies. Quintilia^ recites a worthy
history, of a famous clearke named Hippias, Hippias.
who to avoid idleness, after long studiyng at his books, would exercise him
self in some thing or other, lest he seemed to be idle, in so much he applied
his mind to diverse faculties, at void hours, he used to practice the
faculty of a Goldsmith, of a Tailor, of a Shoemaker, that at length he became
his own Tailour, his own Shoemaker, yea, to make his own rings so
artificially, with pearls and precious stones, so curiously wroughte with
letters of gold, as though he had been brought up in the school Pirgoteles.
What is so hard, but diligence will try it? What is so deep, but trauaill
will wade? What is so strange but study will know it? If labor, if diligence
be thus commended, that wisemen do much commend the Be, that is so busy
and careful, and knows how to profit her self and others. If the little
Auntes be so practiced, for that the toil in the Summer, to provide against
Winter. If these selie simple worms, do provide things necessary for them and
theirs. How much more ought man, which is borne to profit his Country, his
prince, his friends and his parents. Consider the commodity of diligence, and
the danger of idleness but as before mentioned, vic^s are covered with names of
virtues, as the Idle man is noted to be a quiet man, the ignoraunte termed an
innocent. Caelus does write of a certain Emperor named Attalus, Attalus.
which loved so well idleness, that he gave the government of the Empire, to
his friend named Philopenes, for that he would be idle. We read again of one
Vatia, a great ruler and a Magistrate in Asia, that loved Idleness so well,
that the people used a Proverb, when they saw any man idle, Lucinius. Vatia.
saying: Vatia situs est, here is an idlescholer of Vatia. The Emperor
Licinius and Valentianus, were such enemies unto learning, so ignorant were
they, as Egnatius does report, that they called learning the only poison of
the world, and named them that were learned, the Asses of Cuma. Who hated
learning so much as Heraclides, Heraclides.
and Philonides which are so ignoraunte, that they were coumpted, as Caelius
does testify, as laughyng stocks, and had of the common people in great
derision, for their ignorance and folly. These blind baiardes, and bold
bedlemes, call others Asses of Cuma, when that they themselves are far
inferior to any Ass of the world. For diverse Asses had more reason then
Philonides, Philonides.
or Heraclides had. We read that Ammonius a great Peilosopher of Alexandria
had an Ass, that would accompany with Origin, and Porphiri, to frequente the
school of Ammonius, to hear him read Philosophy unto his scholars. This
Ass was taught to know the reader, and all the schoolers, to know the Schole
and the time of readyng. The sacred Scripture commends unto us the Ass of
Balam, who was likewise taught to speak and to show the Prophette Balaam the
will of God. But the Idle and ignoraunte, will neither learn to know time,
place, nor person, neither to profit them selves, nor others. These lasie
members, these idle and ignoraunte beasts, Morpheus the God of sleep.
the children of Morpheus, slepyng always in the cave of Pamedes, to whom it
well may be spoken, as Aurelian sometime an Emperor of Rome spoke unto one
Bonosius, that he was borne to drink, and not to live. The Romans hated
idleness.
The Romans used to punish idleness so sharplye, that the Housebande manne,
that had his ground barraine, that had his Pastures, Meddowes, fields
unoccupied, an other manne should be there placed, and he put out. The
gentleman that had not his horse ready, and in good likyng, with all things
thereunto belongyng, should be suspected to be an idle member unto his
country, should be hated and exchued of the people. The common people might
use no kind of private pleasure, as play, pastyme, or any other idle sport,
Gellius lib. 3. Capi. 2.
but at times appoincted. The gates of Rome were opened day and night, to
come, and to go. For the state of the Common, and as Plutarch does write, the
life and manners of all men, were diligently examined, whether they lived idle
or no. And if any would resist the ordinance of the Magistrates, his head
should be cut of, and offered unto Jupiter, in the Capitol of Rome, his
family unto the Temple of Ceres, his soonnes and daughters, sold as bondmen
unto the Tribunes and Censers. Lacedemonias.
The Lacedaemonians people, most studious to expel Idleness, brought their
children up always in hardness, to practice them in diligente doings, hated
Idleness so much, that if any in the City of Sparta, waxed gross or fat,
they suspected him straight of Idleness, and if any young manne waxed fat,
they had appointed laws that he should fast, and live so poor, until he
were again changed unto his first state. The Aegiptians.
The Egiptians aunciente people, when the Country of Egypt began to be
populous, to avoid idleness, as Plini does report, they made a great huge,
and monsterous buildyng called Pyramides, which for the mightiness and
strange workyng thereof, it was named one of the seven wonders, having lx.
thousand of young men, and continued a long time in the making thereof, and
only to avoid, and banishe idleness. The Athenia^s.
The Anthenians so abhorred, and detested idleness, when a certain man was
condemned to die, for that he was found idle in Athens, a citizein thereof
named Herondas, as Plutarch does testify was as desirous to se him, as though
he had been a prodigious monster, so strange and so maruailous was it to
hear, or to see any idle man in Athens. The people called Massilie^ses,
Massilienses.
would suffer no trauailers, neither Pilgrim, nor Sacrificer, nor any other
stranger to come within their City, lest under color of religion, or of
pilgrim, they might corrupt the youth of the city, with the sight thereof
to be idle. Indians.
The Indians had a law made by their wisemen, named Gimnosophist, that after
their meat was set on the table, the youth should be examined, what they had
done for their meat, what pain, what labor used they that whole morning
before, if they could make accoumpte of their trauaill, they should go to
dinner, but if they had been idle, they should have no meat, without they
deserved the same, with some kind of exercise, either of body or of mind. The
like did the young men of Argis, Argiues.
made accoumpte unto their Magistrates, of their occupations and works, of
their trauaill and pain. Ariopagites.
Even the Areopagites, as Valerius affirms, did imitate the Athenias in
making decrees, in setting of orders, in commaundyng their youth to avoid
Idleness, and exercise travail, then most necessary unto any common wealth,
the other most dangerous. So that some Countries are naturally given to
trauaill, as the Lidians, Phrigians French men, with others. Some again given
to Idleness, as the Persians, Corinthians, Englishemen with others. Some by
law forced to fly idleness, some by punishemente feared, some by death
enforced to labor for their living. Thus this Monster Idleness is beaten
every where, and yet embraced in most places, every man speak against
idleness, and yet a number is in love with it, magistrates and officers
appoincted to punish it, but yet they after favor it.

Of wrath and anger, and the hurts thereof.
THe famous and noble Philosopher Plato, did charge his Scholars always being
in anger or wrath, to behold them selves in a glass where they might see
such alteration of countenance, pale in color, tremblyng hands, foltred
tongues, staring eyes. In fine void of wit, deprived of reason, and being
before reasonable men, now brutishe beasts. Wherefore that great Philosopher
perceiuyng the furious and hasty nature of Alexander, Alexander.
wrote from Athens unto India, where this noble conqueror was at wars with
king Po^us, to take heed of wrath and anger saying. Anger ought not to be in
any Prince toward his inferior, for that may be mended with correction, nor
toward his equal, for it may be redressed with power, so that anger ought
not to be but against superiors, but Alexander hat no coequales, yet in vain
was Aristotle's doctrine unto Alexander in that point, for being in a banquet,
when Clitus his dear friend and foster brother, commended his father king
Philip of Macedone to be the worthiest and most renoumed prince then living,
Valerias. lib. 9. Cap. 3.
Alexander waxed upon a sudden so angry, to prefer any man before him,
though Philip was his own brother which was commended, and Clitus his
especial friend that did commend him, thinkyng rather to deserve praise at
Alexander hand, then to spead of death was thrust unto the heart with a Spear.
So hasty was this prince, that Calisthenes and Lismachus, the one his
philospher and councelour, the other his companion and friend, for few words
spoken either of them slain. Silence says Aristotle is the surest reward unto
a prince. And being sorry afterward and angry withal, yt he had likewise killed
himself, had not Anaxarchus ye philosopher stayed and persuaded him. Tygranes.
We read that king Tigranes of Armenia whom Pompeius the great did conquer,
after waxed so angry by a fall from his horse, because his son was present
and could not prevent his fathers fall, thrust him in his anger with his Dagger
unto the heart. Anger in a prince says Salamon is death, terrible is the
countenance of a king when he is oppressed with wrath, hurtful unto many,
odious unto all is the anger thereof. Nero was so furious in anger, Nero.
that he never heard any thing if it were not to his liking, but he would
requite one way or other with death, in so much in his rage and anger he
would often throw down Tables being at dinner, cups of Gold wrought with
pearls against the walls dashed, fling meat and drink away, more like unto
a furious Gorgone of hell, then a sober Emperor in Rome. Such fury reigns
in anger, Orestes.
that Orestes Agamemnons soonne slew his mother suddenly in his wrath
Clitemnestra. Such madness reigns in anger, Ajax Thelamonius.
that Ajax Thelamonius that famous and valiaunte Greek, after that Achilles
was slain in the Temple of Pallas by Paris, at the destruction of Troie, waxed
so mad and angry because he might not have Achilles harnes, which was given
before to Vlixes, that he beat Stones and blocks, fought with dead trees,
killed beasts, thinkyng to meet with Vlixes amongst them. If anger make men
murderers, if wrath make me^ mad without wit or reason to know themselves or
others, Plato.
let them imitate Plato in his anger, which being angry with any of his
scholars or servants, would give the rod to Xenocrates to correct them, for
that he was angry the learned Philosopher misdoupted himself, that he could
not use modest correction, (...).
even so Architas would always speak unto his servants which had offended
him. Happy art thou that Architas is angry, lettyng his man understand how
dangerous wrath is, for as Aristotle says, the angry man sees not the thing
which lies under his feet* Augustus.
Agustus Caesar Emperor of Rome desired Athenedorus a philosopher of Greece,
which a long time accompanied Augustus in Rome, and now ready to depart
unto Athens, his native zoile, of some sentence that the emperor might think
of him. The philosopher took a pen, and wrote in a little Table this
sentence. Caesar when thou art moved to anger speak nothing until thou haste
recited the Greeks Alphabets, a worthy lesson and a famous sentence, well
worthy to be learned of all men. There is nothing or what can be more vgglie
to behold, more terrible to look unto, then man's face when he is angry, and
the more to be feared for that he has no rule over him self. All the painters
of Persea had much to do to draw in colors the terrible countenance and
fiery face of Queen Semiramis, Semiram^s.
who like Maegera or Medusa grime Gorgons and frettyng furies of hell, when she
heard that her City of Babylon was besiedged of the enemies, being then
dressyng of her head, came with hears hangyng and fleeyng in the wind half
amazed of the news unto babylon, whose ugly and fearful image, most like
unto her at that time stood as long as Babylon continued as a monument and a
terrible mirror to marvel at. We read of the like history of Olimpias,
Olimpias.
whose anger was such when she thought of her son Alexander, she straight
ways like a ragyng Lion or a cruel Tiger, dug up the body of Iola
Alexanders taster, who was thought to be one of the conspiratours, and being
dug up, teared his body in small pieces, and being torn in pieces, gave it
to the birds of the air. Marcus Antonius.
Such anger was in Marcus Antonius toward Cicero, that he was not contented of
Ciceros death, but commanded his head to be set before him on the Table,
to feed his wrathful heart and greedy eyes, Fuluia.
and his wife Fuluia showed her anger, pulled out his toungue, pinned it unto
her Bonnette, and wear it on her head, in token and open show of her cruel
and Tigrishe heart. The noble roman Maetellus was much inflamed for to show
such hatred and anger unto Pompeius, for at what time Pompeius the great was
appointed by the Senators of Rome, Maetellus.
to succeed Maetellus in his office of proconsulship in Spain. Maetellus
perceiuyng that he was discharged, and Pompeius charged, they brake for very
anger all the furnitures of wars, he destroyed all the victualles, he
famished the Elephantes, he permitted his Soldiers te do what injury they
could against Pompeius, so much was his anger against Pompeius, that to
hinder only Pompeius, he iniuried his native city of Rome. The propretie of
anger is, to hurt diverse in seeking to offend one. As he is not wise that
can not be angry, so is he most wise that can moderate anger. The fame and
renoume that both Themistocles and Aristides, in vanquishyng their anger one
toward an other, for being sent both as ambassadors for the state of Athens,
trauailyng over a high hill, like wise men that subdued affection, and
conquired anger, Themistocles said unto Aristides, shall we both bury our
anger in this hill, and go as friends and not as enemies, and there though the
cause was great, at Athens they became friends one unto an other, forgetting
and forgeuing one another fault. Anger and wrath are the only poisons of the
words where hidden hatered does proceade, for to norishe the one is to feed
the other. Therefore it is written, that hidden hatered, private wealth, and
young men's counsel, has been the very cause of diverse destructions.
Manlius Torquatus, after he had conquered Campania, and triumphed over the
Lateus, retournyng unto the City with noble fame, and renoumed victories,
though the Senators and Elders of the City, Val. lib. 9. ca. 3.
met him in a triumph and honor of his victories: yet the young men of
Rome more disdainefull, then courteous, more odious then loving, more willing
to have his death, then desirous of his life, kept them rather his enemies
lurkyng in Rome toward him, then friends, the cause is known in Valerius.
Hidden hatered, which bear swaie in diverse places, envy and malice which
proceed from anger, and maintained with hidden hatered, is all the mischief of
the world. I will omit to speak of Caligula, Caligula.
whose anger and hatred was such, that he wished Rome but one neck, that with
one stroke he might strike it of. Neither I will recite Heliogabalus, which
emo^gest writers, is named the beast of Rome, and not the Emperor of Rome. The
histories of Catelin, Silla, and Appius, for their hatered and anger toward
their country, and native city, are extante in Plutarch and Salust, by this
anger and wrath, proceeded inuectiues and decleratio^s, and then envy and
malice, began to build their bowers, by their chief Carpe^ter anger, then
one mischief and vengeance, does always depend of the other. And because
anger is the only cause of all evil and mischief, I will speak of those two
