of Syon, for that they walk in their own ways, for he has no pleasure in
man's legs, nor in any outward painted show, but in the root of the humble
men's heart is his dwelling. Atchidemus the son of Agesilaus, being
convicted by Philip king of Macedonia, understanding that he waxed proud
thereof, and gloried much of the victory, wrote an Epistle unto Philippe,
saying: If thou measure thy shadow now, being a victour, with thy shadow in
time paste, when thou waste convicted, thou shalt find no longer, now then
in those days, this Prince was wont always to taunte Pride, in so much he
esteemed a proud manne, as a Gorgon, or a Ciclop, or deformed Monster, who
perceiuyng an old man named Ceus, coming unto Lacedemonia, to be very
proud in his apparel, gesture, countenance, aidyng Nature, and setting
forth him self unto the vttermoste, his head being white, he could not tell
how to help it, but to satisfy his proud desire, he died his hears yellowe,
unto whom Archedamus said: O Ceus art thou not contented, that thy mind is
infected with false colors, but thou must have the hears of thy head also.

Gf Coueteousnesse.
Covetousness the privy searcher of hidden gains, the gredie gulf of ill
gotten goods, most painful in sekyng, and most careful in keepyng, whose
one day of death, is better tha^ all the days of his life. The only misers
and wretches of the world are they, Auarus men the only misers of the world.
whom neither shame can reprove, for that they are impudent, neither reason
rule in that they are unsatiate, neither death may fear, in that they think
to live for ever. For even as the infected member of man, is vexed with an
itche, Comparisons.
is always clawyng, so says Plutarche, is the covetous mind restless in
seeking. As fire is never sufficed with wood, nor earth with water, so the
auarous is never satisfied with money. Like as the grave is open to receive
dead Carkases, even as hell is never full, so is the coffer of the covetous
never contented. Alexander most desirous of glory and worlds.
After that Alexander the Great, had conquered all Persia, Greece, India,
Scithia, all Asia, almost all the world brought unto subjection. And having
a cause to come to the School of Anaxarchus the Philosopher, who affirmed by
the authority of Democritus, that there were diverse worlds, which when
Alexander heard, he began to weep, and being demanded the cause thereof of
his counsailers, answered, O Anaxarchus, are there so many worlds to be had,
and I skant have half one world yet. O unsatiate desire that could not suffice
him with all the kingdoms of Macedonia, nor satisfied with all the world, but
wept and cried out, because he might not possess more worlds then one. The
like history of Pirrhus king of Epire, after diverse good success of fortune,
could not suffice his gredie desire with a kingdom. This prince had an
excellent orator named Cineas, which for his eloquence and wit, king Pirrhus
did often use to send as Ambassador to the Romans, Plutarche lib. 27.
to the Macedonians, and to other countries, with whom he had then wars: By
this Orator the king was wont to speak, that he got more countries, cities,
and triumphs by the eloquence of Cineas, then ever he wanne by his force of
wars. This Cineas perceiuyng the king to be very covetous, and most
desirous of wealth, in so much that he longed sore for the spoil of the
city of Rome, considering the wealth of the Romans, he said to king Pirrhus
after this sort: What if Fortune would send, and God permit you to be king of
Rome? What would you the^ do, Pirrhus said Italy is a fertile country, and
full of wealth, I should sone subdue Italy, if I were king of Rome. Then
Cineas demanded again, what after you had Rome and all Italy, would you do?
Pirrhus answered, there is a famous Isle called Sicilia, adioynyng hard unto
Italy, very populous and rich, meet for the king of Rome, Cineas asked the
third time, what then will you do, Pirrhus said, being king of Rome, of
Italy, and of Sicilia, I would sone subdue Carthage and then might I well
conquer all Libia. Cineas being almost weary in demaundyng this coueteous
Prince, the end of his desire, asked the fowerth time, what would king Pirrhus
do then. The king answered, all Greece then should be at my commandment.
Cineas understanding, that there was no end of his unsatiate and greedy
mind, asked of King Pirrhus what if you were lord over all the world, Pirrhus
said then I and thou would be merry, and would live at rest. So there is no
end prescribed to the desire of the auarous, M. Crassus.
until he has all him self. Proved by Marcus Crassus, the wealthiest and the
coueteous Roman that ever dwelte in Rome, so wealthy was this man, that he
adjudged no man rich, Cice. lib. offic. parad. 6.
but he that might with money, keep an army of soldiers in the field, so
covetous was this Roman again, that he was not sufficed with all his huge
wealth, and monstrous riches, but thursted for more, in so much that after he
was slain amongst the Parthians people of great wealth having knowledge of
his greediness to gooddes, they melted gold, in reproach of his avarice upon
his head, and willed him to drink his bealie full, of that which he long
thursted for. Galba.
Even as the head of Galba, a coueteous Emperor sometime of Rome, was smitten
of, and filled full of gold, and offered at the Sepulcher of Nero, in obloquie
and slander of their filthy and greedy lives, both for that which the
covetous man, does honor a live the same, dishonors him being dead. The
Subject that is auarous, is perilous to a Prince: And the prince that is
covetous, is odious to his subjects. Acheus.
Acheus a wealthy king in Lidia, taryng, molestyng* and always most cruelly
vexyng his subjects, waxed so hateful to his own subjects, that through his
auarous dealyng, by popular sedition was murdered, and hanged over the River
Pactolus, with his head douneward, where gold was so plenty, that the waves
thereof offered Sands of gold, in token he could not moderate his desire a
live, he was set being dead, over the golden River Pactolus, to feed his
avarice. What greater infamy can happen to a Prince, then coueteousnesse.
Darius most auarous to open Semira. grave for money.
King Darius having obtained the City of Babylon, through the falshode of
Zopirus, possessyng all the wealth, substance, and treasures of the kingdom
of Persia, having all the spoil of the city, readyng the Epitaph of Queen
Semiramis, which she caused to be set on her grave, to try only coueteous
Princes that should succeed her, she made to be written this little sentence.
What King or Prince so ever thou art, wantyng gold or silver, open my tomb
and thou shal^e find to suffice thee. Darius, I say, not contented with all
the kingdom of Persia, caused the grave to be opened, sought and searched
every where within the tu^be, until he saw written in the inside of the stone
this verse. O thou wicked and wretched prince, if thou had not been most
coueteous, thou had never opened Graves, to come to dead folks for money.
The like repulse had this Xerxes, Xerxes.
King Darius soonne, after he made the grave of king Belus, to be opened for
money, Aelianus li. 13
and finding nothing but the like sentence, written on a short Table, as his
father king Darius found before in the tomb of Semiramis, which sentence
said, that he which should open king Belus grave, and would not fill that
glass with oil, which was in his grave, should have an evil end, which
happened to king Xerxes afterward, for that he was coueteous, to spoil quick
and dead for money, and not so liberal as being a Prince, as to fill up king
Belus glass with oil. Thus auarous princes, sought for money with dead men.
There is no respect to place with the coueteous man, sith he has no regard
to any person for money sake. L. Septumileius corrupted with avarice.
What respect had L. Septumilius, to his assured and dear friend C. Gracchus,

he murdered his friend for money, he lugged his friends body, round about
the streates of Rome. O how infamous art thou Septumilius for thy avarice.
What respect had Ptolomeus, to his singular and often approved friend

head for money unto Caesar. Appianus li. 2*
What regard had Polimnestor king of Thracia, to his friend and nigh kinsman

cousin Polimnestor, Polimnestor.
for old acquaintance and affinity, with great substance and wealth of

to possess the wealth, which Polidor had. Oh unhappy metal that thus move
men to tyranny. O wicked money that make men murder friends. O most cruel
coueteous, to persuade men to betraie men, to kille father and mother, to
deceive Cities and Countries, to forsake faith, L. Silla passed all men in
covetousness*
to subvert kingdoms. L. Silla a most wicked and coueteous, far to be
hated and lothed before Crassus, that wicked and auarous Impe of Rome, passing
all men, both in wealth and coueteousnesse, that in so much he became
through greedy desire of his usual mind so cruel, that he was called an
other fire brande of Italy, such tyranny proceadeth from his coueteousnesse,
that not only in foreign Countries, became a cruel enemy in spoilyng and
robbing, but also in Rome and Italy, his native zoile, a terrible tyraunte,
burning and murtheryng, both Countries and the people. This Silla could never
have to suffice his mind, until at length he was consumed with lice him self,
as his money was corrupted with Mothes. They enjoy nothing, though they have
all things, they possess nothing, though they be Lords of Countries, true is
that golden sentence of the Stoickes, that the wise manne is only rich, for
contentation is perfect wealth. That wiseman Bias, at what time the City of
Prienna was besieged, and subdued by the enemies, the Citizeins thereof
commanded, and suffered with bags of their treasures to depart, every manne
loded him self with silver and gold, but Bias who took nothing but a book in
his hand, and being demanded of the enemies, why he carried not his goods
with him, he said, that all his wealth and richesse, was in his heart,
meaning knowledge and wisdom, a place most fit to lay, and to keep
treasure, saying, I have all my wealth about me, which you can not spoil me
of. Herodotus a noble Historiographer, writes a noble history of one
Alcmaeon, Alcmaeon.
which because he was wont to welcome often the Ambassadors of Cressus king
of Lidia, as they went to Delphos, to consult with the Oracles of Apollo, was
thus rewarded of King Cressus, for his clemency therein, that Alcmeo^ should
have as much gold and silver, by the kings treasurer, as he would be able
to carry, or bear away, which delited so much the coueteous mind of
Alcmaeon, that he was as willing to take all the substance of the King, had he
been able, and being so heavy loded, that his bodily strength failed, to
suffice his gredie mind, would in no wise deminishe any part of his desire,
though he could augmente the ability of his strength by any means, and so
to satisfy his auarous mind, he could never lode him self with a fit
burden, either to heavy that he could not carry, or else to light that he
would not carry, and thus as one not so able in strength as he was willing in
mind, could not prescribe measure to his desire. Had Alcmaeon learned of
Plato, how to deminishe rather desire, to increase his strength, then to
study for riches, he had been able to carry a good burden of gold from king
Cressus Had Demonica learned that lesson of Socrates, Demonica.
that money and desire of wealth is dangerous. At what time Brennus the
Captain and Prince over Senona, laiyng siege to Ephesus, she had not straight
consented, as one more covetous unto money, the^ faithful to her country,
betrayed the City for money, who according unto promise, demanded of Brennus
her deserved reward, brought her to a great heap of gold, and waiyng her
importunate desire unto money, loaded her so heavy with gold, that she died
under her burden, for want of equal strength to her coueteous mind. The
golden Sands of Tagus, all the glisteryng gemmes of Ganges, all the treasures
of Hydanspes, can not suffice coueteous Princes. Such inordinate desire of
wealth, of glory, of fame, reigning in Princes from time to time, that diverse
dreamed in their sleep, how they might satisfy their greedy guts in the
day time, toumblyng and tossyng their brain, how they might possess
kingdoms and Countries. Darius dream
For Darius dreamed that al Macedonia should be at his beck, and that

time did serve king Ca^bises. Alexander dreamed that Hercules appeared to him
every where, Alexanders dream.
and commanded him to pass unto Tire, and to the Isle of Pharo, promisyng

that a young manne of wounderfull beauty, appeared unto him in his sleep,

coueteousnesse. Thus by dreams did they conjecture, how they might be famous,
glorious, and renoumed. Thus through fond fantasies of visions, did at
length become murderers, and tyrants of the world. What was the only
death, of that noble Trojan Hector, the greedy desire to spoil king
Patroclus* What was the end of that renoumed Greek Achilles, his auarous
dealyng with king Priamus; for his soonne Hectors, avarice never lodges but
with princes, noble men, and rich men, the poor of necessity is enforced
to seek his living. Midas history
King Midas was not contented with all the kingdom of Lidia, made his desire
and wish of GOD Bacchus, to be a mean unto Jupiter, that what so ever he
touched, should be gold, which being granted, even at diner his bread that
he touched, became gold, his drink he took in hand, converted unto gold,
and being privy to his misery and wretchedness, that he was in almost
famish^e himself, for that he could not digeste gold, as well as meat and
drink, which could not perceive his estate before, and suffice him with all
things, being the wealthiest king that ever was in Lidia, now through his
coueteousnes, enforced by hunger to die. O hongrie panches of gold: O cursed
desire of money. What has not been done by thee? What place was not sought by
thee? Euriphile.
Euriphiles was so coueteous that she betrayed her own housebande Amphiraus,
to Adrastus king of the Argiues, for a bracelet of gold, that the king did
wear about his arm. Ochus.
If Ochus king sometime of Persia, had observed the rule of noble Plato, which
said, that a king which deliteth in avarice, can not prospere. He had not
stayed from the City of Babylon, for sparing of some silver, which he should
give to the poor women of the city, for it was a Law made by king Cirus,
that what king so ever of Persia, had comen unto Babylon, he should give a
piece of gold, unto every poor woman in the town, for the which cause king
Ochus would never come unto Babylon. These commodities follow coueteousnesse,
toil and trauaill in gettyng, care and thoughts in keeping. Had Euclio thought
of that saying of Plutarchus, Euclio.
that riches for the most part, are hurtful to those chiefly, that do
desire most, he had not hidden under the ground such Treasure and wealth of
gold and silver, that he durst not go out of his house, for fear of
robbing, nor yet as Plautus says, abide in his house, for fear of killyng.
Uerie well is it described of wisemen, the chariotte of coueteousnes, the four
wheels named faint courage, contempt of God, forgetfulness of death, and
ungentleness, the two horses that drew the Chariot are named rauine, and
nigardshippe, the driuer of the horses, is called desire, the two cords that
rule the horses, are appetite to get, and fear to forgette. A whole volume
might be written upon these names, but sith the most part know well the
manner, and motion of avarice, superfluous it is to commente, or gloss to the
smalleste noumber. The Princes are not ignorance, for slaughter, murder, and
tyranny teaches them the order of this chariotte. The noble man desires
always, to ascend unto higher honors, and dignities, by sundry study and
seache of coueteous desire. The gentleman in heauyng his rentes, in spoiling
his tenauntes, in taking all from, and giuyng nothing unto. The merchants
attempt the slipperie Sands of Sirtes, they sail over Charibdus and Silla,
through surgyng seas, to hasarde life for the Pearls of India, and thus all
for money. O wicked world, what profited Cirus to be king of Persia, Ciru^.
of great fame and glory, and then by coueteousnesse, to be vanquished by a
woman, Tomiris Queen of Scithiae, and two hundred thousand soldiers beside
him, his head being smitten of, and cast unto a barell of blood, to drink
that, which avarice compelled him to attempt. What did it avail king
Antigonus, Antigonus.
to name him self the Rasor of Asia, the Shauer of Macedonia, preferryng his
own unsatiate desire, Alexander the great.
before Alexander the Great, upon a brag of his avarice, boastyng Alexander
to be but a Reaper of Asia, and Antigonus the sharp shauer, and spoiler
thereof, the one poisoned in Babylon, in the midst of his triumphs, and
left thirty days unburied, without regard or honor, yea, without any to
put in the ground: The other slain and cut in pieces, emongeste his
mortal foes, Alexander king Pirrhus soonne. What should I recite sondrie
Princes, private persons, wholly addicted to this vile avarice, but Kingdoms
and Countries, altogether drowned in this miserable Coueteousnesse.
Lacedemonia^s.
The Lacedaemonians, the renowned people of the whole world, after their long
banishemente of money and manly contempt of wealth, Lisander.
at length corrupted by Lisander to embrace coueteousnes in Athens, Athens.
the sacred aucthor of learning, the Well of Philosophy, after they had
despised King Philip of Macedonia, and all his wealths in fine brought in
subjection, by money valiant Thebes while Spaminondas lived subject to no
desire, Thebes.
desirous of no riches, but yet allured to yield to money, in so much they
waxed so covetous, they began one to war against another, that in seeking
to be Lords and chief one over another, they became slaves and subjects to
foreign princes, which all the while they were not moved by covetousness, they
resisted the violence of their enemies, but even as Aesopes dog was not
contented with the meat that he had in his mouth, but being greedy to snatche
at the shadow thereof, he lost that which he carried in his mouth. Haniball.
Had not Hanniball been covetous of Rome and Italy, he had not lost Carthage
and Libia. Pirrhus.
Had not Pirrhus been desirous to possess Macedonia, he had not been
dispossessed of Epyre. If Mithridates had been contented with ye whole kingdom
of Pontus. Mithridates.
If the great king Antiochus had not thursted for Aegipt, Antiochus.
he had not been banished from Assiria. Such coueitousnes grew on a sodden in
the city of Rome, that Iugurth king of Numidia would often say that Rome
would have been sold for money. If Biers and Sellers could have happened to
me. So covetous waxed Rome, that all things were soulde for money, in so
much that when Antiochus had prepared a great army sumptuously appointed,
with Helmets, Spears and Targats of Silver, and demau^dyng of Hanniball
whether he was well appointed to fight against the Romans, Haninball
understanding the wealth and substance of the Soldiers of Antiochus one way,
and not ignorant of Romans force, for that he.xvj. years had famous wars
against them, perceauyng the desire and greedines of the Romans to
substance, another way answered king Antiochus after this sort. There is
sufficient at this time for the Romans, Rome famous
though they be ever so coueitous. O Rome the Lamp and Lantarne of the world,
while money was not known. Sparta renowned.
O Sparta most renowned, while yet the laws of Licurgus was observed, when
hard Iron was coined for money, then Rome flourished, then Sparta prospered,
then Romans were feared, and Lacedaemonians dreeded. O golden world, before
the coin of Iron, when shamefastnes ruled the people, and justice governed
princes. It is seldom seen when wealth increases that avarice abateth, for as
the world grew unto wealth, so it grew unto covetousness, when gold was found
first by Cadmus in Mount Pangaeum, Cadmus found first money in Mount
Pangaeum.
as both Pliny and Herodotus affirm, then was no earth dug, no Seas saild,
no countries travailed unto for money, but now they have dug unto the very
bottom of Caucasus, unto the depest part of the earth, and like to dig
Pluto out of hell, if they continue says Phalerius. There is no dangerous I Will
on earth, no gapyng Gulf in Seas, but money has sought it out. Would to GOD
some ofspringes of king Agis, Agis fire.
who sometime in Sparta commanded all the rich Citizins to bring their Books
and Bills of accoumptes unto the common Haull, where and when he perceived the
Lacedaemonians to be much given unto avarice, the City of Sparta unto usury,
he called for all the bills of deptes, the creditours, assuryng themselves of
paime^t, was rewarded after this sort, al their bills and books of deptes
burned before their face, with a full sentence of the king and the wisemenne
Ephori, Agesilaus was wont to speak, that king Agis fire was the best that
ever was in Sparta, far better then Neros fire, who burned and would have
often flamed the noble city of Rome, where he was Emperor and governor of,
Agis burned the scroules of Usurie and coueitousnes, in respect of his tender
love toward the people of Sparta. Nero set afire the whole City in
consideratio^s of his hatred toward his own Subjects, trustyng more the
spoil and the wealth of Rome, then ready to govern the state of the Empire
with wisdom and virtue. King Agis exclamed with famous Maro, against money
and coueitousnes, the Emperor Nero, did always cry out and procure that
verse of Horace, saying. O Citizins, first seek money and then trauaill for
virtue. Plini. 33.
There should no stranger inhabit Sparta that was either rich or desirous of
riches, but when they began to embrace wealth, to love money, to wear Golden
Ringes on finger, Pearls hangyng at their ears, Bracelettes about their
arms, Chains about their necks, at length they wear Gold and precious
stones on their shows, then wantonness and lechery were first chambered, then
glotonie and dronkennes were fostered, then vices and wickedness began to
prevail, Virtue and Godliness began to be oppressed. Thus Avarice first began
by princes, and maintained by all sort of men, Desire of fame.
then of covetousness sprung up desire of fame and glory, not only in Kesars and
Kings, but in mean and simple persons, how simple a slave, a vile abject,
most desirous of fame. Heroaeratus.
Herostratus by name burned the great Temple of Diana, coumpted one of the
seven wonders for her monstrous and huge buildinges, detected himself of the
crime, to become famous. Pausanias a mean man in respect, Pausanias.
demaundyng of his friend Hermocles, how he might be famous, was councelled to
kill king Philip, king of Macedonia, father unto Alexander the great, which
as willing he did accomplish as he was desirous of fame. Xenophon.
Xenophon a noble and a wise Philosopher, perceauing his wife named Aspasia,
more given to avarice, more alured with gourgeous sightes, then others were,
having opportunity demanded of his wife, if her neighbor had better apparel
then she had, which would she have if she might change, his wife said her
^eigbours. Again Xenophon merrily disposed, would know of his wife if her
neighbor had more Gold, Silver, Treasure, or wealth would she change if she
might, to whom she answered most willing she would, what said Xenophon if
your neighbor has a finer fellow to her housbands then you have, would you
change with her, silence with shame was her answer. Thus Xenephon tauntyng
his wife with such nippes, brings me in memory of one Pythius, Pythius.
borne in Lidia, and commoraunt, in a City of Phrigia called Caeleius, a
notable rich manne and not so rich as coueitous, toilyng and tramplyng all
the day time in pain and travail, seeking and searchyng money, tomblyng and
tossyng all the night time, restles without sleep, thinkyng always on money,
his wife understanding his grief and want of rest. At what time Pithius her
housbande had appointed certain strangers to come to dinner, to prove whether
they might being his gestes make him merry or no, caused and willed his wife to
make such good cheer for him, as would make him merry and his gestes, his
wife understanding that nothing might make her housebande merry but money,
prepared the Tables ready, furnished them over all with Gold and Silver, his
house hanged with clothe of Arreis, every place settled with wealthy
treasures, which when the Gestes came, they were amazed of so much Gold and
silver, dismayed quite at the gorgeous sight and wealthy Tables. Pithius
feedyng himself a long while with the sight thereof, enamored with the portly
shows of Gold and Silver called for meat, his wife before all the strangers
being bidden Gestes by her houseband, answered her housba^d that she provided
for no meat. Pithius being angry with his wife, said unto his wife, what have
I willed you this morning to do, to provide said she such cheer as could
make you merry, where is it said Pythius, behold said his wife on the tables
there is king Midas dinner, these things housebande do make you most mearie
and nothing else do you exteeme. Certain Xenopon the great Philosopher tanted
no better his wife Aspasia, then this seely and simple woman nipped her
housebande Pithius. Gold is the delight of the auarous manne, spoil and
catching still is the desire of the covetous. All is fish that comes in his
Net, Adrian.
as by that auarous Adrian surnamed Sophista, proved when a gentleman a
neighbor of his had sent him a few dainty fishes for a present, covered
very fair on a great silver dishe, he took both the silver dishe and the
fishes, saying to the messenger thank thy master, and tell him I take his
fish for novelties and dainties, and that I take his silver dishe for a gift
and a present. Such impudency reigned in covetousness, that like ravening
Harpeis, they spoil rob and catch every where such greedines lurk in
avarice, that like hongrie dogs they devour any thing. The poets feign that
Jupiter though he be king of kings, Jupiter contented with the Sky.
is yet contented with the Skies. Neptune with the Seas, Eolus with the Lands,
Pluto with Hell. If the Poets affirm that immortal Gods live by measure,
contented with Lottes, agree with Fortune, why are Kings not sufficed with
Kingdoms, Lords with Lordshippes, men mortal never satisfied with any
thing, how greedy of Gold, how desirous of Silver, how covetous of countries
the wealth of some, the penury of others can well declare it. This private
wealth apply the place where avarice is honored, the blood of the covetous is
his money, to seek to borowe or to ask money of the covetous man, is nothing
else but to crave blood out of his body, for sooner says Plato shall a man have talk of a dead man, then receive benefits of the covetous man. The
Purse of the covetous is shut up and sealed, his hand is ready to take, his
Cofer to receive. Simonides.
Simonides at what time any man would demand him to write, to read, or to
pleasure the poor any way for thanks, he would answer that he had in
his house two Cofers, the one shut up for thanks, the other open always for
money, in the one said Simonides I find somethyng always, in the other I
find nothing at all times, he heald his hands ready to take with Vespasianus,
Vespasianus.
sometime in Rome an Emperor, when he had hard by his friends, that a silver
Image of great substance should be made for a monument of his worthiness, in
memory of his chivalry, he straight healde out hand, saying: behold, here is
a place ready to set the Image, a sure foundation from falling. The coueteous
Prince, was more greedy to have the money unto his own purse, then to thank
his friends for their love, and good will in honoring him, with a perpetual
monument. How happy judge they them selves to be, that are wealthy, and how
contemteously despise they others that be poor. We read that Craesus a
wealthy king sometime in Lidia, Craesus thought him most happy by wealth.
ioyed so much of his substance and riches, that he thought no man so happy
as he was. This king to brag and to boste of his gold and silver, thought
good to send for Solon the Sage, and wise Law maker of Athens, to show unto
Solon his fortunate estate, which being demanded of Craesus, when he came to
Lidia, whether any manne in the world, was in better estate then he was:
answered, that a poor Greek called Cleobes, was far more fortune then he
was: Cresus being dismayed, and half angerie with Solon. Demanded again, who
was of like Fortune, or whether any man was coequall in wealth unto him. Solon
preferred before him again, for Fortune and wealth, one Biton. The third time
he asked the like, Plutarchus in vita Solonis.
and Solon commended before him one Tellus. Then king Caesus commanded Solon
to depart his presence, with great wrath and anger, and where he had
thought to give him plenty of gold and silver, if Solon had preferred his
fortune: he gave him great checkes, showed him hidden hatered, that he
despised his estate, and wealth. But at last, this worthy and rich king,
being convicted, and taken in wars by Cirus, when that he should die, at the
solempnitie of his funeral, he thrice called Solon happy, for that Solon
despised most, which he honored chief: That name which I had thought to
claim to my self happy, by my wealth, I surrender it to him most worthily,
by dispraisyng of the same commended me unto Solon, said he a little before he
died: and tell him that now I am satisfied with death, which could never be
content in life. Giges.
The like history of braggyng Giges, who only for such a purpose, went unto
Apollo to Delphos, to know by Oracles, whether any manne in all the wide
world, was as rich as he, and there after long bragges of his Kingdoms, and
vain ostentations of wealth: an answer was made unto him by Apollo, out of
the secret place of the Temple, that Aglaus a poor manne of the Country of
Arcadia was far richer, and more fortunate then he was. Thus are they most
misers, noted of wise men, having all things, and yet having nothing for that
they are never contented with any thing. When Alcibiades had much bragged of
his fortune, wealth, and substance, boasted very much of his lands in
Athens, a certain Philosopher showed a little Table, wherein the whole
Country of Athens was described in a very little room, desiryng Alcibiades
to show him there his livings and lands in Athe^s, which when Alcibiades
might not, Alcibiades.
nor could in any wise show the same: No more said the wise Philopher, can
any coueteous manne show any part of his substance, for that they have
nothing, though they have much. Thus Craesus and Giges, though they were most
wealthy kings in Lidia, yet were they both by Apollo and Solon, adjudged
misers. So odious was usury and coueteousnes that when it was demanded of
Cato the wise, what usury was, he answered, what is to murder a man? It
robs and kills poor men, it murthereth innocents. This is that false
fellow that will sell all things with Simo^. This is he that will betray Christ
with Judas. To this it is said, that sooner a Camel shall go through the eye of
a Nedle, then he should go unto heaven. Wherefore the memory of death is
better, to him that has pleasure in possessions.

Of Prodigality and Dronkennes.
THE great delight, the Epicureal felicity that Princes have had in excess
of eating and drinkyng, from time to time, in all places, are not only in
profane Histories regestred, but also in Divine Scriptures* mentioned. The
Poets make songs of prodigal Princes, the Histriographers defame them, the
Philosophers abhor their company, how could the Poet Sidonius omit the
prodigal draught of Cleopatra Queen of Egypt without a song, Cleopatra.
how might Pliny forget the sumptuous excess of the Empress Poppea without
a taunt. Poppeia.
How should Martial let pass the impudency of proud Bassa, Bassa.
without worthy scoffs of her too much prodigality. The first preparyng a
banquet to her friend and lover Antonius, where one dishe of meat stood her
in two hundred thousand Crowns. The second so sumptuous and prodigal, to set
shows of glisteryng Gold under her Horses feet. The third most proud and
impudent to appoint vessels of Gold, wrought with Precious stoanes, to receive
her excrementes in stead of her stool of ease. Their sumptuous prodigality,
excess and pride must not be unspoken, to show the horror thereof, as an
example to other proud prodigal Princes. Therefore to match these sumptuous
Ladies, I will travel no further then the city of Rome, where three Emperors,
one succeedyng in a manner the other, not only comparable to these dainty
dames, but far surmountyng them in each respect. Caligula the only Dregs
and Faex of Emperors and Princes for prodigality, Caligula.
and excess spending and spoilyng the whole revenues of the Empire of Rome,
upon Hoores and Queanes, a Sacrilege of churches, a spoiler of the City, a
robber of all the Country, so filthy was this Emperor, so odious for his
excess, that he wished that all Rome had but one neck, that he with one
stroke might strike of the head, to the end he might have to suffice his
prodigality, his Acts perticulerly to touch it were tedious, for he never did
a good turn to the City of Rome. The second Nero, Nero.
surnamed the tyrant Caligula his sisters son, most like his own son,
for that he was known to be naught with Agrippina his sister Neros mother,
this Emperor past Caligula his uncle, in all excess of glotonie and
dronkennes, wasting and spendyng with strumpets and queanes, consuming day and
night with riotous and infamous persons, using the company of Minstrels,
frequentyng feastinges and banquets, accompanyng the fellowship of Thieves and
Tyrauntes, that Nero himself was called the firebrande of Rome. The third
Heliogabalus, Heliogabalus.
the very sincke of shame, the only rotten member of the world, who past all
the slaves of the world in prodigality, tearyng all to pieces in bravery,
Precious clothes, commaundyng Nauies to sink before his face in the river of
Tyber, his house with all precious odours and sweetness, his Galeries strowed
over with Saffron, his Stool of Gold, his Chamber pottes of Onix stoan, his
Slippers wrought with precious Margarites. I will omit to speak of his
monstrous lechery, of his cruel tyranny, of other prodigious and terrible
acts, but let the learned read Suetonius of Heliogabalus life, let the
unlearned judge of his life by his death, who being kilde on a Iakes, and
thrown to the River Tiber, and lest any ofspryng might succeed him, the
Citizins of Rome through his mother Scemides alive to bear him company, for
that she brought up such a Gulf of mischief. I am amazed to thynk wherein the
wide world I might find his mate, because that all writers do agree, that in
excess and drunkenness only, Alexander the Great.
for that fault Alexander the great, the conquirour of the whole world, is
much defamed. I am urged amongst so many virtues, that Alexander had this
only filthy and abominable vice to note wherein he had great delight, making
certain garlands and brave Crowns of Gold, appointyng great gifts and rewards
for them that excelled in drinkyng. Calanus when he should die at the gorgeous
pomp of his funeral, and solemn exequies, he desired Alexander to make a
memorial of his death, by some sacrifice of drinkyng, Calanus Dirige.
which Alexander accomplished, made three Garlands of pure gold, the best
valued lxxx. pounds. The second.xxx. Pound. The third.x. Pound. And then
prepared a sumptuous feast, with such Diriges of drinkyng for Calanus, that
Alexander wan the best and first Garlande. Promachus the second* The third a
hundred made claim by law. Thus Alexander having such felicity in
drunkenness. Androcides a gentleman of Greece wrote unto Alexander being in
Babylon, perceiving the prone and propensed mind of this Prince to win, a
letter, wherein was a Table written one with this little Sentence in letters of
Gold. Remember Alexander when thou do drink wine, that thou do drink
the blood of the earth, he never hurted any but in his wine, famous in all
things, infamous in that, exteemed and honored of all in all things, lothed
and abhorred in that thing in all the world. Such cruelty he showed in
drunkenness, that he slew his own sisters brother Clitus, killed Calisthenes
his philosopher and councelour, murdered his dear friend Lisimachus. Cambises
king of Persea, Cambises.
and Father unto Cirus the great, such tyranny he would commit in
drunkenness, Seneca lib 3. de ^ra and Brolonius lib. 1. ca. 5
as being either spoken or counceled, he would reward with death and
torments. At what time Praxaspes, one of his councelours, willed him to
abstain from wine, which makes Princes not only subjects to infamy and
reproache, but would also deprive Princes of senses, declared the propriety
of wine unto Cambices, as to make the feet to stamber, the eyes to stare, the
hands to shake, and all the wit of man to be banished. The king commanded
Praxaspes to bring his only son, which he had before him, which being
brought, he took him by the hear of the head with the left hand, and his
dagger drawn in the right hand, saying Now Praxaspes thou shall know whither
my feet will sta^ber, or my eyes stare, or whither my hand will fail of thy
sons heart, and with all he thrust Praxaspes soonne unto the heart. So
dangerous is dronkennes, so filthy and beastly is this Uice, that Cleomenes a
famous king sometime of Sparta, Cleomenes.
being much given to imitate the dronken Scithians, and Thracians people of
shameful life, became from a stout and a strong king over the famous people of
Lacedemonia, to be a Rascoll and belly slave, subject unto all Greece. Suetonius
does make mention of two prodigal brethren, either of them then named
Vitellius, whose sumptuous estates of filthy life, with such enormitie of
drinkyng and excess of glotonie, that almost comparable they were to that
monstrous Beast of Rome Heliogabalus. What infamy is, or can be more unto a
man, much more unto a prince then dro^kennes: When Philippe King of Macedonia,
and father unto Alexander the Great, had heard that a certain Macedonian had
appealed from him unto king Philippe, he demanded of the manne what king
Philippe was he that he did appeal unto. The manne answered, I appeal from
king Philippe the drounkard to day, Philippe.
to king Philippe sober to morrow: Yet this king for all this taunte, commended
the boldness of the woman, revoked his false sentence with shame, being
given before when he was in wine, and Judged right being sober. Too true is
that sentence of the wiseman, that who so ever he be, Keisar or King, that
thinks to drink always, and not to be dronken sometime, let him then
think to drink poison, and not to die of it. Beasts never drink says
Plini, but when they are thurstie, A log will be dronken. The Raven. The
Olephant and the Ape. Aelianus. 2.
manne only bibbyng always. The Dog will be dronken, if he drink wine,
and the Rauons likewise, if they taste the same. If the great and huge
Elephant drink wine, he loses straight his strength, knows not his force,
and is easily convicted. If the Ape drink wine, she forgets her pastyme and
tricks, and can do nothing but sleep. And therefore naturally all beasts
abhor wine, as enemy unto their strength. Drunkenness is a privy
murderer, that spoileth life on a sudden. Archesilaus.
Archesilaus as Hermippus does report, Domitius.
and Domitius surnamed Apher, which Eusebius makes mention of, in his
triper^ite histories, the one by superfluous feedyng, suddenly at supper
choked: The other Caroussyng a draught of wine, died presently. This was so
abhorred of the wise, that when king Antigonus came kissing and clipping of
Zeno the Philosopher, whom the king so loved, that he willed Zeno to demand
what he would, and he should have it. Zeno perceiuyng the state of king
Antigonus, took him a side, besoughte him in his ear to go vomit, and to
purge his stomach, whence such sent and smell proceaded, that Zeno could not
abide the breathe of king Antigonus. Antiochus.
King Antiochus of Siria, having wars with the people of Rhodes in Chalcides,
by occasion of wine and drinkyng, fell in love with a young maid of the City,
consumyng a whole Winter, drinkyng and feastyng with glottonie, and
drunkenness in the day time, wantonnyng and chambring with her the night
time, neglectyng greater affairs, whereby honor and fame might be gotten, to
obaye bealie, and to accomplish lust, whereby infamy and shame grew unto
him. These greedy gluttons want not their parasites, to flatter them in their
drunkenness, to feed them in their vicious and filthy life, yea, to
maintain them to do evil, and to let them to do good. Had not Dionisius
the tyraunte Aristippus? Had not Caesar Curio? Had not Alexander the Great
Promachus. Yea, even as Terence says, where ever Thrasonicall masters be,
there be also appoincted out of the School of flattryng Gnatho unto these
Princes, glorious Parasticall servants. To this Epicuriall pamperyng of
bealies, to this inordinate cramming day and night. To this continual
feedyng, Tapyrum.
was the people of Tapirum, a nation so much given to wine, Aelianus. 3.
that when they could drink no more wine, they wash them selves over naked
with wine and oil, then they went to sleep until they were able again to
drink. Byzantias.
To this drunkenness the Bizantianes were so addicted, that they sold their
lands, set out their houses, to apply the Tauernes, leauyng their wives as
common to others at home, as wine was to them at Tauernes, forgettyng all
honesty of life, mindful only of glottonie and drunkenness. Argiues and
Corinthians dronkardes.
The Argiues and Corinthians, says Maenander, people beyond measure given to
prodigality, of drinkyng, banquettyng riottyng and feastyng, to one from an
other, that in surfette of eating and drinkyng, they end their s^launderous
life, with reprochefull death. O brutishe and beastly life, to hurt that at
all times, that sought all men's distructions, always to live in shame, and
to die in misery. The law of Pyttachus.
The law of that wiseman Pittachus was amongst the people of Mittilena that
glotto^s and dronkardes, should be double punished. For as Laertius says,
when they do evil, they blame the drink, and so excuse one fault with a
greater fault. The saying of the Emperor Aurelian, unto the great drinker
Bonosus, that he was borne to drink, and not to live, may be well applied to
a great noumber of this age. The Scithians and the people of Thracia, The
^cithians, and Thracians most given to wine.
the welspringes and parents, whence all drunkenness and glottonie proceade:
much like unto the Agrigentines people, always in great excess, in so much
says Plato, that they build continually, as though they should live for
ever, they eat and drink day and night always, as though they should die
the next day. The people called Batilli annointe them, and wash them with
wine, in and out, even as says Plini, the Ciprians and Parthians do, which
are so dry, that the more they drink, the more thurstie they are. What should
I recite the Beotians* which were noted of the learned Athenians as rude
blocks, witless people, and gross fools, for that they were greedy to
glouttonie, and drunkenness. But speak of the aunciente Greeks, which did
mingle their wines with Oil, to make it sweeter, whereby they might drink the
more, Athens corrupted.
and carouse the better In time Athens it self, as Aristophanes bears
witness, grew unto such daintenesse of feeding, and prodigal clothyng,
Aelianus. lib. 12.
in so much that Isocrates did compare Athens, unto a curious harlotte, who
would much rejoice of her brave apparel, sekyng and alluryng every manne, to
fall in love with her finenesse, that Diogenes the Philosopher, going from the
City of Sparta unto Athens, being demanded on the way, by certain
gentlemen of Greece strangers, whence he ca^, and whether he would go,
answered and said, I came from men, and I go unto women: Notyng the
valiant hardiness of the Lacedaemonians, and tauntyng the daintiness and
finenesse of the Athenians. Rome prodigal.
This prodigality of eating and drinkyng, was so honored, this finesse of
feedyng was so accepted, that a little fish, says Plutarche, was more
accepted in Rome, then a great ox. What wickedness proceeded from dronkennes?
What lust moved dronkards. Secundus.
Secu^dus the Philosopher being dro^ken, did lie with his own mother, as an
ignorant beast by wine, being a learned philosopher, which she knowing being
sober, slew her self therefore, and he for shame of the act, and for sorrow of
her death, pined and tormented him self, all the days of his life. The huge
and mighty Holifernus being drounken, Holifernus.
was beheaded by Judith a selie woman. The monsterous Ciclops Poliphemus,
Poliphemus.
being overcome with wine, sleping in a cave, was slain by Vlixes a simple
Greek. The pugill and Champion Alexander, Alexander.
the conqueror of all the whole world, was betrayed and murdered with wine.
If such reproach happen by wine, if such perilles and dangers accompany
drunkenness. If sudden death always wait for glottons and dronkardes, hence
marvel much men may, that all men every where at all times, do embrace
this prodigious monster. Lacidis.
If Lacidis the Philosopher fell into a palsy, by drinkyng of wine, and by
drunkenness died. Why did Mar. Antonius make a book in defense of wine, M.
Antonius.
to maintain his dro^kennesse, which book was as odious unto Rome, as
drunkenness was esteemed of Antonius. If Philostrates by drinkyng of wine,
Philostratus.
was made dro^ken, and brake his neck from a ladder, by a fall, to his great
infamy and shame: Timocreon.
Why did Timocreon cause an Epitaphe to be set on his grave in Rhodes, for a
brag of his ignomious life, saying: Here lies Timocreon of Rhodes, that in
life time eat much meat, drank much wine, spoke much evil. Thus vice
begins sweet, it ends sower: It begins with pride, it ends with
shame: It begins with wealth it ends with poverty. Licinius Crassus.
Even as Licinius Crassus was flouted sometime in Rome, scofte in Italy, mocked
of all men, because from a wealthy man, he became a poor beggar, by prodigal
expenses. So are they laughed to scorn as bealie slaves, waied as infamous
persons, regarded of no man, that are compted dronkardes. Even as Gnosius was
contempned in Greece, Gnosius.
that the Citizeins of Athens commanded their children, to avoid his
company, for that he was noted such a glotton, and a dronkarde, that when he
went in the street^ every one pointed his finger at him, when he would go to
any house every manne would avoid his company. Plini does repete the
benefits of dro^kennesse. Plini. lib. 24. Cap. 2^.
Thence says he, proceed the dropsy, the swollen visage the pale color, the
queueryng hands, the foulteryng tongue, staring eyes, read nose: hence says
Plini proce^de furious dreams, stinckyng breath, and beastly behavior: hence
the gout and palsy, and all kind of diseases, these come of immoderate
drinkyng of wine, modesty in drinbyng of wine, reuiueth the spirits,
comfort the heart, sharpeneth the wit, and makes glad the heavy mind.
Homer.
As Homer the sugered and sweet Mecaenas of Greece, was much revived by a
draughte of wine. The aunciente Poet Enneus, could best apply his study
after a cup of wine. Aristophanes and Cratinus, felt them selves most apt,
and ready to versifie after little wine So that Pithagoras said true, that the
Uine has three granes: the first of pleasure, the second of drunkenness, the
third of madness: The first draught is pleasant, the second is dangerous,
the third is madness. Some write that it has seven granes: the first as
before said, of pleasure, the second of company, the third of farewell, and
thus free, then the fowerth of slander, the fifte of braulyng, the sixte of
blows, the seventh of madness: then it weakeneth the senewes, it kills the
memory, it dulleth the wit, and spoileth all the sense of man, and of a man makes a beast, vomityng and spuyng both wine and the secret, for in
wine there is no silence. There were certain soldiers in Tarentum, Tarentum.
a city sometime under the Romans, and all that time defended by Pirrhus
king of Epire, who being drounken, and ouercomed with wine, began to slander
and to defame Pirrhus with obloquie, and ignomious talks and being demanded
by Pirrhus, Pirrhus.
why they so spoke of him, one answered and said, more had been spoken, had
not wine wanted. Three things there are that much are to be lamented: the
mariner in a tempest, the poor manne in debte, and the prodigal person with
money. Thus Demosthenes would often repeat in his Orations, to move the
Olinthians and the people of Athens, Polido. lib. 3. Ceres in Sicilia honored
by Triptolemus.
to abhor prodigality and drunkenness. There was sometime a Temple in
Sicilia, consecrated unto Ceres, where Triptolemus appointed solemn sacrifice
great honor, with glottonie, and access of eating and drinking. Mela^pus
likewise as Herodotus does write brought first from Egipte unto Greece, the
excess order of drinkyng* called the feast of Bacchus, from Greece not long
after it was brought unto Hetruria, from Hetruria unto Rome, from Rome unto all
the world. Thus glottonie and drunkenness grew, hail fellows with Princes.
Aelianus lib. de vary histo.
In Pontus with king Mithridates. In Lacedemonia with Cleomenes. In Sicilia
with Dionisius. In Lidia with Cambetes. Plini lib. 14. Cap. 22.
Timolaus in Thebes, And as Plini affirms, in Greece with Alcibiades and
Diotinus: In Rome with Piso and Torquartus. In Aegipt, Herodotus prefers
king Amazis. These princes and noble fellows were Soldiers under the banners
of Bacchus and Ceres, maintainyng glottonie and drunkenness, two vile vices,
for two noble virtuous. Worthy were the people of Sparta and Lacedemonia, of
immortal commendatio^s of perpetual fame. Plutar. lib. 4^. Sparta.
They so abhorred this vile vice of drinkyng, that they made their slaves and
captines named Helotes, at their feasts appointed dronken, that their children
might see the beastliness thereof, that they might mark the shameful state
of drunkenness, thereby rather to abhor the filthiness and brutish behauour of
dronkards by using their household servants, in so much that if any of their
children waxed fat or gross in Lacedemonia, they should fast it out with
bread and water. Wherefore Anacharsis the Scithian, being demanded how a man
might exchue dronkennes, answered in beholding the uncomely gestures of
drunkenness, for some dronkards will brag much of their drinking, and not be
dronken, Aristippus.
as Aristippus hearing a certain Greek boastyng much, that he could drink
much and not be dronken, said even so can a Mule likewise, co^paryng his
great bragges to the beastly Mule. Uery fittly does Cirus touch his grandfather
Astiages, when he was asked how it chanced that he quaffed wine no better,
because said Cirus I fear to suppe up Poison with wine, which spoileth the
right use of body and mind. We use to excuse dronkards sometime being
wisemen, even as Demosthenes excused king Philip of Macedonia, of whom
Philocrates and Aeschines, after they had returned unto Athens from their
Legacy, said that king Philip was a beastly quaffer, then said Demosthenes,
virtue has her proper spung, I say no more but that vice has some Bulwarks
and defenses every where.

Of Magike and witcherie.
THE aduenturous searchers, and privy prudent Philosophers, have sought by
influences of the heuens, to bring things fourth like unto the works of
nature as beasts to speak, dead bodies to go. In this faculty some of the
best in every Country travailed, Buda. Hermes. Zimolxides. Numa.
as Buda amongst the Babilonians, Hermes amongst the Egiptians, Zamolxides
amongst the Thracians, and Numa Pompilius amongst the Romans, and amongst
the Perseans was this Magic exteemed; that their Kings therein were
instructed, as an art necessary to be knowem for Princes. For that wise and
learned Socrates went unto Gobrias an excellent Magician, Socrates. Gobrias.
to be taught in this Art, and to learn the punishment of souls, and their
restyng places after death, which was instructed as Crinitus says, in.5. lib.
and Cap. 2. in all things according unto his expectation. Pliny says, that
Moises used a kind of Magic, Moises. Salamon.
which was likewise granted by God unto Salamon, as Josephus in his eight
book of antiquity affirms. Pithagoras. Empedocles. Democritus.
Likewise Pithagoras, Empedocles, Democritus, and diverse other Philosophers
travailed unto the furthest part of the world, to be acquainted with this
faculty, and after forsakyng of their Country, at their return practiced the
same, and opened the hidden and secret nature of the same unto others.
Strange things may be brought to pass by ioinyng inferior things, with
superior qualities, which by curious search of natural and celestial
bodies, their hidden and secret powers are known, and by practisyng of the
same marvelous effects sometime appear, as by this means Architas the
Tarentine made a wodden Dove to flee. Architas wodden Dove.
And Albertus the Great made a brazen head to speak. Lactantius says, that
Appolonius was so excellent in this art, that when Domitianus the Emperor
would have had him punished, he suddenly being before the Emperors face, to
avoid the threatenynges and appoincted punishment, vanished out of sight, by
this art Arnuphus an Aegiptian Philosopher used to fly in the air, Arnuphus.
and made such a wonder in Rome, in the time of Marcus Antonius, when the
Soldiers wanted water to drink, he caused Lightnynges and Thonders to
provide rain, to satisfy the thurst of the Roman soldiers. By this likewise
does Pliny, report of a king sometime in Armenia, which allured more the
Emperor Nero being at supper with him, to be in love with the enticement of
Magic, then to delight in the Harmony and melody of music, and other shows
provided for the increase of mirth, for natural Magic was so exteemed, that it
was thought only to be the profoundnes and perfection of natural
Philosophy, making open the active part thereof, with the aid and help of
other natural virtues. The Indians and the Egiptians, because there was
abundance of herbs, Stones, and such necessaries as might serve this
purpose, excelled in this faculty. And because Astrology has some affinity,
and great conference with Magic it is conueniente in some poincte, to touch
what they have dooen from time to time. How strange was it that Anaxogoras
said, that a great stone should fall from the Sun the second year after.
78. Olimpiade in Egos, a River in Thracia, which came to pass as Plini
report in his second book and sixte Chapter. Pherecides was so skillful in
Astrology, Phirecides.
that when he saw water drawn out of a wall, he said then, that in that
place should be great earth quake. Sulla hearing much of the enormitie, and
lust of Caligula the Emperor sometime in Rome, send him in writing how, and
after what sort he should within sewe days die. Meson.
This by judicial Astrology did Meson, foreshewe to the Athenians, sailing
then unto Sicilia, the tempest that was to come upon the Seas, whereby he
feigned him self mad, to avoid the danger thereof. By this Sulpitius
Gallus, opened the effect of Eclipse of the Moon, unto the fearful soldiers
of P. Aemilius, which feared and doubted, to meet King Perseus and his army,
until they were certified of the cause thereof. The judgment likewise that
they have of men, by their faces, and contemplations, of the proportions of
Nature, iudgyng some to be Saturnist, others to be Marcialist: some to be
Iouialist, others Mercurians, appliyng some unto the Sun, others unto the
Moon: It is written, that the ancient Philosopher Pithagoras would take no
scholar, or admit any to come unto his school, without he judged him apt,
and meet by sight to receive learning. The Kingly Philosopher Socrates, was
judged by Zopirus, to be by Nature a drounkarde, a whoremonger, and most
vicious in diverse things, to the which Socrates by feelyng of the provocation
of Nature, agreed and said, that he was naturally given unto those vices, that
Zopirus judged him. Atlas.
Atlas was so excellent in Astrology, that the Poets do faigne, that he
vpholdeth the heavens with his shulders. Berosus.
Berosus had his Image in Athens, erected and put up of the common people, for
the skill he had in Astrology. Cynop^.
Mirandula does write of a famous Magicia^ in Pathmos named Cinges, which was
wo^t to reise ded bodies, and to work wonders in the time that Ihon the
Euangeliste preached the Gospel of Christ. The Egiptians believed that Amphion
was so connyng in Magique, Amphion.
that the Poets faigne that he made stones and Trees to follow him. These
conjectures which both Magiciens, and Augurers do gather from the heavenly
bodies, upon all inferior living things, by certain signs and tokens,
placed in their mouynges, standing, gesture, and goynges, are nothing else but
to search the hidden force, and secret woorkyng of natural bodies, which
was so esteemed in times past, that the Lacedaemonians did assign an Augurer, to
sit in royal seat in judgment with their kings, and to be every where
present at the Counsel of the City, to decerne things, and to instruct
them therein. The Romans had a whole company of these, ready to teach
them things to come. The Greeks flowed of these, for they took nothing in
hand without consultation, either with Amphiraus, Amphiraus. Tiresias. Mopsus.
Tiresias, or Mopsus. The Phrigians had such confidence in diuinations, that
Ascanius the son of Aeneas, asked counsel of Augurers before he went unto
the field against Mezentius In fine, the Cicilians, the Arabians, and the
Vmbrians followed Augurers devise and cou^sailes, in all their doings and
attemptes, Therefore from age to age, Astrologers were the Keys of the
Augurers, to practice their secrets by erection of their figures, and
conjectures of similitudes, to show the very way unto divination. There is an
other kind of Magic belo^gyng to Witchyng, that is done with charmed drinks,
and medecines, where herbs are most esteemed. As Virgill, of a certain
herb called Pontus, makes mention, how he saw a man named Moeris, changed
hereby unto the likeness of a wild wolf, by such means. It is supposed that
Apuleus could shifce and alter him self when he listed unto the likeness of an
Ass And here I mean to speak of those that were most renowned in
witcherie, I marvel much that women only excelled in this faculty. For
Circes dwelling not far from Caieta a City of Campania, at what time Vlixes
and his men passed unto Phrigia, she charmed the companions of Vlixes unto
Swine, with this went Medea king Oetes daughter of Cholchos, Mexea.
to learn the force and secret virtue of herbs, which excelled both men
and women in Charmyng, for after that she had tamed the Dragon, that kept the
golden fleece, and made him sleep with drink, for the love that she bare unto
Jason, with whom she took her voyage unto Greece, as his wife, and when Jason
came to Thassaly Aeson his father, her father in law being old, she restored
him unto his youth again by her art, notwithstanding, Jason afterward
forgettyng the benefits, received by Medea, in receiuyng the golden fleece, in
restoring his father again unto his former age, put her away, Creusa.
and married Creusa the daughter of Creon King of Corinth, where Medea had
just occasions tendered to work her art. She therefore dessemblyng the
matter, did send three smockes, as a present unto King Creons his wife, and
unto his daughter, which then was married unto her housebande Jason, which
were so Charmed, and enchaunted of Medea, that as soon as the King, the Queen,
and his daughter had put them on, immediately they burned with sudden fire:
she to avoid the sight of Jason, and to augmente his anger, took her two
soonnes which she had by Jason, and hewed them unto small pieces, and so was
carried straight unto the air, and fled unto Athens, where she married
again King Aegeus, Aegeus.
Theseus Father. From thence after that she bewitched Theseus, she fled
through the clouds from Athens unto Asia, with her soonne named Medis* These
and other feats Medea used, Cirses.
whose fame thereupon excelled far Cirses, or Calipso, whose cunnyng were
such, that Cicero doubted whether they were women, or devils, no less
prodigious then cruel. Things are brought to pass by these witches, whose
incantations and charmynges protende always unto great harm and hurt,
Eurephila was such a witch, that if she might se any body with her eyes, she
sought no further to hurt her enemies, then with sight. Which like wise is
written of Medusa, which for her skill therein, any body that had seen her,
should be converted unto a ston. We read of one named Pasetes, Pasetes.
which with his charmed incantations, could make when it pleased him
banquettes of sweet meats to appear before his friends, and when he would he
could make the same to vanishe away. Plini does say in his 28. book,
Exagonus.
that one Exagonus came unto Rome from Cipris, as an Ambassador, who was so
connyng of this art, that he to please the Senators, willed them after he had
long reasoned, of the force and virtue of herbs, to put him, yea, to throw
him headlong amongst a great noumber of Serpents, which were kept in Rome,
to stynge and to punish offenders: which when he was thrown, the Serpents
licked him about the lips, and showed such obedience and love toward him,
as moved much admiration in the Senators. Hercale,
We read that Hercale could at all times, enchaunte wild and ragyng Lions to
be so gentle, that he with his hands, would lead them up and down without
resistaunce. Atir.
We read that Atir as soon as he touched any Dragon or Serpent by incantation,
he would make them sleep. Pithagoras.
We read that Pithagoras used this pastyme, to write with blood in a Glass,
such things as he thought mete, which being turned directly against the
Moon, showed and appeared unto the^ that stood behind, things written in the
circle of the Moon. Hermes.
There are books extant of Hermes, of Numa Pompilius and others, Numa.
which practiced this kind of iugglyng. Simon.
Simon the Samaritan, which for his knowledge in this, had an Image made for
him in Rome, in the time of Claudius Caesar themperor with this inscription is
this picture erected, to Simo^ the holy God. To omit a while to speak of
private persons, which be infinite: but to declare of diverse Countries and
Kingdoms, which were over whelmed in this. Marsi.
As Marsi a people in Italy, which have their names of Marsus, the son of
the Witch Cirses, which studied and travailed in this kind of knowledge, that
they could tame all wild beasts: and make wounders by enchauntementes. Again
the people in Illiria could with sight, those that long looked upon the^,
bewitche unto death, if they hated them. There be women in Scithia, that can
do the like called Vitiae. Vitae:
Herodotus says moreover, that the people called Neuri in Scithia, are so
skillful in this fassination, that they can shift them at will, unto the
likeness of Wolves, wherefore the Persians, having trial in this knowledge,
for that it was much esteemed with them in times paste, had this Law at length
to punish that, Upon a great stone, the head of the Inchauntor, or of the
Witch should be put, and with an other sharp stone, should it be cut in
pieces. Hipparchus did banish Onomacritus out of Athens for that he used these
feats, too much and too often. In this wise men appear unto the enchaunted
eyes monsterous beasts, as Horses, Asses, and such like. There are so many
kinds of enchauntmentes, that some with herbs, some stones, some with
sightes, and some with words charm and bewitche things. As Orpheus with a
certain hymmes aswaged the stormy tempeste, which was like to happen to Jason
companions upon the seas. Vlixes.
And Homer witnesses that Vlixes blood was stented with words. These will
promise any thing to come to pass, that they take in hand, much like unto
that aunciente Serpent the Devil, which promised unto our forefather Adam,
the sciences of all things, which only it was to bring man unto destruction.
And so Coniurers, Magicians, Enchaunters, Sorcerers, and such like, will
promise golden hills, and yet are they not able certainly to perform any
thing. Where these arts first sprang, Plini in his thirty book and the
first Chapter, does at large declare. And most certain it is, that by
art diverse and sonderie strange miracles are seen, and tried, and no less
certain it is, but by the devil the most of their shiftyng shows are
apparaunte. Julius Caesar after he had a great fall from his Chariotte, was
wont to repeat at any time afterward, when he would go unto his Chariotte, a
certain verse three times together, lest the like luck should happen again.
By a little verse spoken does Theophrastus bear witness in Plini, that
Ischiadici were healed. In the same place does Cato report, that certain
words did help Luxatis membris. And Marcus Varro speaks even so of the
gout.

Of comparisons between wealth and poverty.
AN old saying there is, that comparisons be odious emongeste equalles, and
certain more odious is it emongst vnequalles, as between the rich and the
poor: But sith the state of man is so diverse, that some is contented with
little, and some never with much: Some toilyng and tramplyng, having all
things, as though they had nothing. And some again quiet and careless
having nothing, as though they had all things, considering that contentation
of mind, and quietness of heart is the chief felicity, and sovereign good
of the world, waiyng beside that poverty is sure and saufe every where, and
riches unsure and dangerous at all times: And that poverty is careless, and
wealth careful. I see not but by comparyng of the lives of Emperors, Kings,
and Princes of the world, unto selie poor men. But the state of the poor
is better then the state of the rich: as by Apollos Oracle, Aglaus. Cressus.
proved of one Aglaus, a poor manne in Arcadia, preferred for his contentation
before King Cressus of Lidia, which took him self the happiest manne in the
world for his wealth. Darius.
What profited king Darius to vanquishe the Persians, and to be King of
Babylon, and to have out of Asia five hundred, three score and fiftene thousand
Talentes, yearly paid of tribute, for he lost not only all his wealth, but
also his life, for his wealths sake, by Alexander the great. Alexande^.
What profited Alexander again after that, to have the wealth of Persia, the
substance of Macedonia, yea to have all India, and Asia, and almost the whole
world in subjection: and yet to be poisoned of Antipater, and left unburied
without regard, thirty days in Babylon, for wealth was the chief cause of
his death. Was not poor Plautus and simple Cleanthes, Cleanthes. Plautus.
more happy in life, then these two famous Kings: they quietely with bakyng,
and bruing and appliyng their books, lived a long time saufe and sound. These
Princes were never at rest, until one destroyed an other in their youth, and
prime time. King Xerxe^ and Cirus got such abundance of wealth by wars,
such substance, such treasures, that being thereby disquietted, and puffed
unto Pride, to take enterprises in hand, through persuasion of wealth, that
the one after he thought to spoil, and rob all Greece, was slain by
Artabanus, the other thinkyng to subdue all Scithia, was vanquished by Tomiris
Queen of Scithia a woman, and so both these proud Princes of wealth, lost
wealth, life and gooddes. Was not Philemon and poor Acaetes, which lived unto
the last course of Nature, quiet at rest, more wise happy then they. If
quietness of mind, if long years, if sauftie and soundness of life if
perpetual health may persuade felicity of life* If poor Faustulus a
Shepherd, Faustulus.
which sometime found in Romulus, left of all men, forsaken of his parents,
hated of his freindes, and nourished him. This Faustulus lived more merry years
in keeping of his Sheep, a Shepherd, then Julius Caesar did in rulyng of Rome,
Caesar.
an Emperor. For this Shepherde ended his life in old age: this Emperor was
murdered in the Senate house, within four years after he was elected
Emperor. Was not Codrus a poor Poet, Codrus. Procula.
and his wife Procula, more luckie and happy to end their days, after long
life quietly, then king Ninus and his wealthy Queen Semiramis that killed her
housebande, Ninus and Semiramis.
for the kingdom of Assiria, and after she likewise was slain by her own
soonne Ninus, for the obtainyng of the wealth and substance they had left
was lost. Examples are to many for the proof hereof. Solon a wiseman of Greece
did prefer before rich, and wealthy king Cressus, Biton and Cleobis
brethren, Biton Cleobis.
two poor men of Argi^e. The poor family of one Aelius in Rome, where
there were seuentene brethren, having but one poor field for their heritage,
lived longer, proved happier, and died more godly, and lived more co^tentiuely,
then the family of Caesar. What was the cause that the Indians, Babilonians
Arabians, and Lidians were spoiled destroyed, and murdered, their great
substance* treasures, and wealths of the Countries. If we examine well the
doubts and dangers of wealth, and weigh likewise the sauftie and surenesse
of poverty, we must needs prefer the quiet state of poor men (if they be
wise) before the state and Fortune of Princes. The worste luck that can happen
unto a poor manne, is to become rich, for then he comes from sure life, to
hasarde him with death: And the best that can happen unto a rich manne, is to
become poor, thereby is his life saved, though his wealth be spoiled. How
Fortune deals in this poincte, it is in diverse places, of this book
mentioned. Demosthenes. Cicero.
The worst thing that could happen unto Demosthenes and Cicero was, that they
were advanced from their sound state unto honor and dignity. Whereby their
lives were lost, the one being a consul of Rome, the other the patron of Athens.
The worst thing that could happen unto Tullius Hostilius, Tullius.
and unto Torquinius Priscus, Torquinius.
was that they were advanced, the one from a Sheparde, the other from a
banished stranger, to be kings of Rome. If I should rehearse in this place,
how many poor men by calling them unto wealth and honor, have been
banished afterward, slain and imprisoned, both in Rome in Greece, and in all
the world. I might seem well unto the reader more tedious then delectable. If
on the other side I should repeat how many Princes have escaped death, by
loosing their Crowns and Scepters, I were no less werie then fruictlesse.
Therefore I say there is care and danger in wealth, and there is quietness and
safety in poverty. Lucanus.
The pleasure that Lucanus had in this world, was nothing else, but a poor
Garden, where always in his life, he used to solace him self, and when he
died, he commanded his Grave to be made there, Mecaenas.
where being dead, he was buried. Mecoenas had such a Garden in Rome, where
all his feli-Citie was, Octauius.
the Emperor Octauius might in no wise allure Mecoenas out of his Garden,
Alexander. D^ogenes.
no more then Alexander the great could move Diogenes to forsake his Tun to
become wealthy, here again might be brought those wise men, that refused
wealth and substance, fled from honor and dignity, to live quietly in
poverty, but because I mean to be brief in all things, I will omit
prolixitie. Pliny does report, that Protogenes a poor Painter, Protogenes.
was content to live all the days of his life, in a little cottage that he made
him in his garden. Plutarche likewise in the lief of Nicia, does write, that
Lamachus was so poor, Lamachus.
that when the people of Athens would make him their king, he wanted I say
Shues on his feet, and yet had rather be poor Lamachus in safety, then to be
king of Athens in danger. For when a poor man named Hilarion, Hilarion.
met with certain Robbers and Thieves, and being demanded of the Thieves how
chanced that he feared not to walk alone in the night, because said he I am
poor, and therefore I fear no man, who sees not the danger of the one,
and the surenesse of the other, for Arison was wont to say, that poverty was
as it were a Lantarne to behold the wickedness of the world, for poverty is
the true Nourse of all good virtues. Licurgus being demanded of certain
Citizins of Sparta, how might men avoid their enemies, he answered by
poverty, wherefore Licurgus made laws and decrees amongst the Lacedaemonians,
that no Soldiers should spoil the enemies, though they were dead in the filde,
for he feared lest wealth should be the destruction of Sparta, For wealthy
men are envied at and hated, and every where sought by snares to be destroyed,
which poverty escapes. That wise Greek Solon was wont to say, that wealth
was the mother of excess, excess the mother of lust, lust the mother of
violence, and violence the mother of tyranny. Therefore says Pithagoras that
wealth must be ruled with wisdom, as the wild horse is tamed by the bridle.
Simonides when he was demanded, which was better wealth or wisdom, he said
after this sort, I doubt much said he, for I see so many wise men
frequentyng the rich man's company that I know not which.

Of Death.
DEATH is the last line of all thing, the discharge of all couenauntes, the
end of all living Creatures, the only wish of the good, and the very
terror of the wicked. And for that the life of man is diverse, so is Death
variable of sonderie sorts, and fashions, as by experience seen and known in
all Countries. Nothing is surer then death, nothing more variable then Death.
For Pindarus that wise and sage king of the Liricans, Pindarus.
being demanded of certain Beotians, what might best happen unto manne in
this world, even that said he, which chanced unto Trophoniu^, and Agamedes,
meaning Death: For these men after they had builded a new temple unto Apollo,
demanded of God Apollo, the best reward that he could give unto them: they
thinkyng to speed of some dignity, or some worldly substance, were rewarded
within seven days after with death. The like we read in the first book of
Herodote, Biton and Cleobes.
where the mother of Biton and Cleobes, tw^o young men of Argos, kneelyng
before the Image of Juno, besoughte the Goddess to bestow some excellent good
thing, upon her two soonnes, for their pain and trauaill that they showed
toward their natural mother, in drawyng the Chariot ten miles in stead of
horses: but the Goddess willing to show the best thing, that could be given
unto manne, the next night followyng, quietly in bed as they slepte, died.
Wherefore very well did Aristippus answer a certain manne, which asked how
Socrates died: Even in that order, that I wish my self to die* Giving to
understand, that any death is better then life. That noble Philosopher Plato,
a little before he died, as Sabellicus does write, lib. 10. cap. 13.
did thank nature for three causes, the first, that he was borne a man, and
not a beast: the second, that he was borne in Greece, and not in Barbary. The
third, that he was borne in Socrates time, Hermes.
who taught him to die well. Hermes that great Philosopher of Egipte even
diyng, so embraced death, that he called upon that divine spirit, which ruled
all the heavens, to take mercy upon him, being right glad that he passed this
toilyng life. Such is the snare of death, that some in half their days, in
midst of their fame and glory die. As Alexander the great in Babylon.
Pompeius in Egipte, and Marcellus which being a young man of great
towardness, and soonne by adoption to the Empire of Rome, Albius,
died. Even Albius a Roman knight, in flourishyng years ended his race. And
M. Caelius one of Ciceros scholars, a very eloque^t Orator, Caelius.
and of great fame, being in a manner a boie, died. It is strange to see the
shifts of death, in how diverse and sondrie fassions it happened unto Princes
always that some merry in their bankettes, and drinkyng were slain. Clitus.
As Clitus of Alexander the great, being his sisters brother, and his chief
friend. Ammon of Absalon being bidden unto a banquette, Ammon.
was slain of his own brother, yea, all the Ambassadors of Persia were
commanded to be slain, even drinkyng at the Table, by king Amyntas soonne of
Macedonia. Some end their lives wantonnyng with women, and plaiyng in
chambers, as that renoumed Greek Alcibiades, Alcibiades.
being taken in Uenerie with Timandra, was slain of Lisander Eue^ so Phaon
and Speusippus the Philosopher died likewise. Some bathyng them self, were
choked by their own wives: Agamemnon.
As Agamemnon that famous Greek, by his wife Clitemnestra. And Argirus
Emperor of Rome, by his wife Zoe. Diverse in prison as Captives died, as
Aristobulus, Emnenes, Aristonicus, Marius, Cleomenes, Iugurth, Siphax, famous
and renowned Princes. Diverse in Iacques slain, as that beast Heliogabolus, whom
Rome so hated, that he fled unto a Jacques, and there was killed, and after
drawn through the streates, and thrown unto the River Tiber Gneus Carbo a man of great dignity, Carbo.
and power of Rome, was commanded that he should be slain, as he was sitting
on his stool of ease, by Pompeius, in the third time of his Consulshippe in
Rome. Thus shamfull diverse died: and thus famously others died. The snares of
death, the hooks of time, the end of man was always unknown. Patroclus.
Patroclus knew not that he should be slain of Hector. Hector.
Hector never thought he should be killed of Achilles. Achilles.
Achilles never doubted his death by Paris. Paris.
Paris never judged that he should be vanquished by Pirrhus. Pirrhus.
Neither Pirrhus was certain that he should be ouercomed by Orestes, Orestes.
so that no man knows his end, where, how, and when he dies: and yet all
men are certain and sure, that they have an end, that they must needs
die. The fear of death has much overcome the stoutest and worthiest
soldier* We read that Asdrubal of Carthage, Fulgo. lib. 9. Capi. 13.
a noble and a famous Captain a long time, and yet at length being convicted
by Scipio, he for fear of death kneled before Scipio, embracyng his feet and
so fearful, that his own wife was ashamed of his doings. Yet had this noble
Captain rather be a laughyng stock unto the Romans, a bond manne unto
Scipio, runnyng a foot like a lackie before his triumph, then to die manfully
in the behalf of his Country, which valiauntly for a time he defended.
Perpenna.
Perpenna likewise a famous Roman, being taken in Spain after Sertorius was
dead, by the Soldiers of Pompeius, in a wooddie place full of Groves,
fearyng lest at that instante he should be slain by Pompeius soldiers, made
them believe that he had diverse things to speak unto Pompeius, that the
enemies had in hand, to bring to pass against Pompeius: rather had Perpenna
betrayed his friends and his fellows, yea, and all his Country unto his
enemy Po^peius, then that he should be in the fear of death. A greater fear
of death we read in that book of Fulgosius, Vitellius.
of that noble Vitellius, who after he had vanquished and slain diverse nobles,
and showed great wrongs unto the Emperor Otho, and unto Sabinus, brother
unto Vespasian the Emperor, being in fear of his life by Vespasian, and being
taken by the soldiers, he besought lest he should die presently, he might be
kept saufe in prison, until he might see and speak with Vespasian the
Emperor. Such was his fear, that he did hide him self in a chest like a
wretch, lest upon a sudden he should die. So fearful was Caligula, Caligula,
and Caesar of death every where, that he would never go abroad at any
Lightenynges or Thonder, but with his head covered with all kind of things,
which might resist the violence of Thounders and Lightenynges. Misa king of
