the Moabites, and Ioram king of Iewrie, being besieged by the enemies, and in
danger of death, they invented all giles, all policies, and inventions right
or wrong, to save life, they sacrificed their children, to mitigate the rage
of the Gods lest they should die. The love that diverse had unto life, and
the fear the same self had unto death, were to be noted worthily, considering
how sore men are vexed with the fear of death* Antemon.
Antemo^ was so desirous to live and so fearful to die, that skante would he
trauaill out of his house abroad any where, and if he were compelled to go
abroad, he would have two of his servants, to bear over his head a great
brazen Targatte, to defend him from any thing, which might happen to do him
hurt. Theagenes.
Theagenes in like sort would not go out of his house, without he had
consulted with the Image of Hecates, to know what should happen to him that
day, and to understand whether he might escape death or no, Commodus.
Commodus the Emperor would never trust any Harbor to shave his bearde,
lest his throat should be cut of. Masinissa.
Masinissa king of Numidia, would rather commit his state and life unto
Dogs, then unto men, as his guard to keep and defend him from death. To
speak of Bion, of Domitianus, of Dionisius, of Pisander, of thousands more
which so feared death that their chief care and study was, how they might
avoid the same. The fear of death causes the soonne, to forsake the father,
the mother to renounce the daughter, the one brother to deny an other, and one
friend to forsake the other. In so much Christ himself was forsaken of his
Disciples for fear of death. Peter denied him, and all the rest fled from
him, and all for fear of death. Behold therefore how fearful some are, how
joyful others are. How loathsome some, and how willing others have been to die.
Some desperately die being weary of life. As Sabinus Iuba, Cleomenes, some
hangyng the^ self, some burning them selves, and some drouning the^ selves, and
thus with one desperate end or other they died. But fith every man must die,
it were reason that every man should prepare to die, for to die well is nothing
else but to live again. Wherefore certain Philosophers of India called
Gymnosophistae, being of Alexander the great, commanded to certain hard
questions, which if they could absolve they should live, otherwise they should
die, and the eldeste of their company was made a judge in that matter by the
said Alexander. The first question was demanded to know whether there were more
living or dead, Alexanders questions to the Philosophers of India.
to the which the first philosopher said, that the quick are more in number,
for that the dead have no being, no place, no number. The second question was,
whether the land engendered more, or the Sea, to this answered the second
philosopher and said, the Land did engender more, for that the Sea was but a
portion of the Land. The third question was, to know what beast was most
subtle, that beast answered the third Philosopher, that man can not discern.
Fourthly it was demanded why they being Philosophers, were so induced to
persuade the Sabians to rebellion, because said the Philosopher, it is better
to die manfully, then to live miserably. The fifte question was, whether the
day was made before the night, or the night before the day, to the which it
was answered the day. The sixte was to understand, how Alexander the great
him self might get good will of the people, in showing said the sixte
Philosopher thy self not terrible unto the people. The seventh to ask was
whether life or death were stronger, and it was answered life. The eight was to
know how long a man should live, until said the eight Philosopher a man thinks death better then life. The last question was proposed of
Alexander to know how might a mortal man be accounted in the number of the
Gods. In dooyng greater things said all the Philosophers, then man is able to
do. For that they knew this proud Prince would be a GOD, and that he would
learn of the sage Philosophers how he might void mortality, he was answered
sharply, for that he should know him to be a man, and being a man, he
should make himself ready to die, for death is the reward of sin, and death
is the beginning of life again unto the good, as Aulus Posthumous, Aulus.
in an Oration which he made unto his Soldiers, said, It is given both to
good and bad to die, but to die Godly, and gloriously, is only given unto good
men. For so Hector speaking in Homer, said unto his wife Andromacha, that she
should not be sorry for his death, since all men must die. Though some with the
Galathians do contempne death, that they fight naked, and are persuaded with
the Pythagoreans, that they shall never die, but passing from one body unto
another, yet some die joyfully, as the brethren of Policrita, being taken
captive by Diognitus* Emperor of Milesia, she being used very ill at the
Emperors hands, did send Letters to Naxus, unto her brethren, at what time
the people of Milesia were Feastyng, Drinkyng, and Banquetyng at their great
solemn feast. Then the brethren of Policrita came and found the Emperor
drinkyng, and all his people over charged with wine, slew the most part of
them, prisoned a number, and brought their sister home to Naxus, where as soon
as they came home died, for Joy of the victory. Even as Philarchus sometime in
his great triu^phe criyng out. O happy hours and ioifull days, was taken in
such a Fever, that brake his veins at that very instaunt for gladness. He is
coumpted most wise that knows himself. To joy to much in prosperity, to
be advanced and extolled when fortune favors, without all fear of ill happes
to come is folly. To be vanquished and subdued in aduersite, without hope of
solace and goodness to come, is mere madness. Therefore the Sages and Wisemen,
knowing that death was the last line of life, did endeavor in their lives, how
they might die well, and briefly for examples of lives, I means to note few
sentences of these wisemen, which they used as their Poesies, and thought good
to show their answeares unto diverse questions unto them propounded.

Bias dwelling in the City of Prienna, after the city by Nutinenses was
destroyed, Bias escaped and went to Athens, whose Poesy was Maximus improborum
numerus, he willed all young men in their youth to trauaill for knowledge, and
commands oulde men to embrase wisdom. This Bias being demanded what was
the difficulst thing in the world, he said, to suffer stoutly the mutabilitie
of fortune, being demanded what was the infamoust death that might happen unto
man, to be condemned said he by law, being asked what was the sweetest
thing unto manne, hope said he, what beast was most hurtful amongst wild
beasts, a Tyrant said Bias, and amongeste tame beasts a flatterer, and
being demanded what thing it was that feared nothing in all the world, good
conscience said he. And again in the second Olimpiad the Philosophers
demanded other Questions, as who was most unfortunate in the world, the
impacient man said Bias: What is most hard to judge, debates between
friends, what is most hard to measure, he answered time, that having fully
answered to diverse other questions, Bias was allowed one of the seven wise men
of Greece.

Chilo the second of the Sages being borne in My^tilena, being asked what was
the best thing in all the world, answered, every man to consider his own
state. And again being demanded what beast is most dangerous, he said of
wild beasts a Tyrant, of tame beasts a flatterer, being asked what is most
acceptable unto manne, he said time, and being asked of the Greek Myrsilas,
what was the greatest wonder that he saw, he said an old man to be a
Tyraunte, these with diverse other questions was he asked of the Greek, his
Poesy was N^ quid nimis.

The third was Chilo the Lacedemonian, being demanded what was a difficult
thing to man to do, he answered, either to keep silence or so suffer
injuries, what was most difficulst being asked of him, he said, for a man to
know him self, and therefore he used this Poesy, Nosce teipsum. This Chilo
being of Aesopus demanded what did Jupiter in Heaven, he said, he does throw
down lofty and proud things, and he does exalte humble and meek things.
Solon the Athenian had this short Sentence in his mouth. Nosce teipsum, know
thy self, for in knowing and considering what we are, how vile we are, we
shall have less occasion ministered unto us, to think well of our selves, for
there is nothing good nor bewtifull in man, as Solon being asked of king
Cressus, sittyng on his Stool of state with princely Robes, bedect with
Pearls and Precious stones, whether ever he saw a more bewtifull sight then
King Cirus sittyng in his majesty at that time, to whom Solon answered and
said, that he saw diverse birddes more gaie to behold then Cirus, and being
demanded of Cirus what birds were they, Solon said, the little Cock, the
Peacocke, and the Feasaunt, which are dect with natural garments, and
bewtified with natural colors. This Solon was wont to say, I wax daily
old, learning much, he noted nothing so happy in manne, as to live well,
that the same might die well, appliyng the cause unto the affect, as to live
well, then to die well. If I should molest the reader with the sage sayings
of Cleobulus, Thales, Periander, and others tending only for the amendmente of
life and readiness of death, I should seem tedious, here were a place to induce
diverse and sundry examples of death.

HAd Greek Calisthenes silence kept, had Neuius spared speech,
Had Theocritus busy brain, offended not his leache:
Calesthenes had not lost his life, nor Theocritus died:
Ne in Maetellus wrath, so long had Neuius poet abide.
The soundst reward, the surest gift should Memmius have in the end,
Had he to Caesar nothing said, that Caesar might offend:
But as I fear Chirilus stripes, and dread Aristos draught:
So with Antilochus to write I am, to some Lisander taught.
Some carpyng Crete, some peuishe Pan, and some of Colax kind,
Some of Gnatho school will scanne, some fla^trie here to find.
I will not haulte with Clisophus, I love not Curi^ style,
I hate Philoxenus forged faith, Aristippus phraise to file:
But with Sinaetes persean poor, with Cirus water crave,
Her princely pardone on my knee, with Cirill Poet have.
Who to avoid Charibdis gulf, I fall in Scillas band,
To seek to shun Semphlagades, I sink in Sirtes sand:
With wearied wings of Icarus, with Phaoetons charge in hand,
Most like Actaeon bound before, her noble grace I stand.
No Momus may Minerua sail, no Phaaeton Phoebus charge,
No wilful wings of Icarus, may Dedalus flight discharge:
Therefore yea Gods that guide the globes, the glisteryng glaryng sky,
The whirlyng spheres, the firmamente, and poales of heavens hie.
You starrie states and imps of Jove, your graces three attend,
Approach in place Pierides, my vain in verse to bend.
Each pilgrim Prince in prose is paste, each Queen must now in vearse,
Have honor due and fame deserude, the heavens hie to pearce.
Whose praise shall pearce the clusteryng clouds, and skale the empire sky,
Whose thunderyng clanges of bruted fame, on earth shall never die,
Each passing pearls Prince in place, from stools of states redounde.
Whom birds abroad on brakes do brag, their praise in skies to sound.
Who^ whirlyng winds and whispering woods, who^ brauling brok^ aduau^ce,
Whose ecchos shrill of fliyng fame, through surging seas do lance.
Who ruled people proud and fierce, and nations stout subdued,
That widows were, and virgin^ Queen's, with wisdom great endued
Who reads not Zinobias fame, who does not Mesa know,
Who hears not of Sabas name, that any where does go.
What worthy acts, what famous feactes, what virtues rare were seen,
When noble King Mausolus died in Artemesia Queen,
The noble Queen Semiramis, King Ninus famous wife,
Did rule Assiria saffe and sound, when Ninus lost his life.
When Constant Emperor died* his wife no less there love did l^ue,
Then pearl Penelope had in Greece, or Roman Lucrece have.
Amongst the Illireans to Teuca then, such worthy name did chance*
As in Arcadia Atlanta did her noble fame advance.
Queen Dido ruled Carthage cost, Helerna Tibur brave,
As sometime did Cloelia, the Roman scepter save.
Why seek I thus to shun the snares, and shifte with verses often,
Sith praise of force must press the place, where wisdom rules a lofte.
A Prince of port in silence kept, that does expecte the end.
Whose rule and royal race by course, needs not in books be pende.
In whom the Muses build their bowres, the graces make their forte,
With whom Sibilles sages sit, and sacred Nymphs resort.
Who Judith like with threatnyng swearde, Holofernus mates to spoil,
A second Susan sure she is, all Judges false to foil.
An other faithful Sara sad, with Aesters mace in hand,
In prinsely place Rebecca, like to rule her native land.
She in triumphant seat does sit, with Laurel leaves be decte,
With Olive branches brave on head, that do his fame detect
This to the Muses nine a tenth her grace, did Jove betake,
And with the Graces three, a fourth Apollo her did make.
The fame Aurelia had in Rome, by Caesar first did spryng.
The like Olimpias had in Greece, by Alexander King.
Who had known Cassandana, had Cirus fame not wine,
Or who had heard of Creusas name, had not Aeneas bin.
The force that Hector used in field, extolde Andromachs name,
The port that Traiane Emperor had, advanced Plotinas fame,
But she that has no sire no soonne, a second Delbora is,
To sit in royal room to rule, in joy and state of bliss.
Who is by Jove appointed, from divine Camaena school,
Like sad Cassandra for to sit, in sage Samberta stool,
Who with Euphem^s fostered was, in heigh Latonias tower,
Whose cradle long Melissa rockt, within dame Venus bower,
In sad Sibillas bower brought up, of sacred Vaesta taught,
Corinna learned though she was, her service Sapho sought.
Give Saba place, Zonobia yield, behold in place such one,
That learned Ladies link themselves, her grace to wait upon.
Whom Prudence teach, and Justice rule, whom temperaunce does profess,
All virtues rare and godly gifts, her noble mind possess.
Whom Gods by birth with one consent agreed her name should be,
Pandora for his galaunt gifts, in glory great to glie:
Whom if Cornelia were alive, and hard of this her fame,
She with Hortensia would from Rome, to England viage frame.
The haughty dames in warlike weeds, those Spartan Ladies lo,
And Mars himself with Thracia^s Queen's, to this would yeald I know
In Phrigian fields, in Trojan camp, was Penthisilea seen,
With strong Achilles force, in field to fight like valiaunte Queen.
In Rutill land on Turnus side, against Aeneas lo,
Came Queen Camilla armed to field, the Trojans force to know.
The Scithian stout Queen Tomiris, that bloody Cirus slew,
With CC. thousands soldiers slain, which Persea long did rue.
That noble Queen Antiopes, and Hippolite in band,
Durst both Alcides rage endure, and Theseus stout withstand.
Thus that women were such wightes, to fight in field with hand:
In Saca, Scithia, Cimbria zoile, and in Lacedena land.
Though spears and shield they shook, though harness they did bear,
Yet far inferior to this Queen, in Marshall feats they wear.
Minerua she from turrettes high, with regal Juno state,
Unto the sacred Nymphs above, elected sovereign mate.
With princely Perseus Pollax lo, she slew Medusas brood,
She cursed Cacus lincke did cut, with strong Alcides mood.
With sage Vlixes swearde I say, she Poliphemus slew,
And she with mighti^ mace of Jove, the ymps of hell subdue.
She dasseled Argos hundred eyes, and does Zopirus seed,
And shall each virtue plant in time, and vice in time out weed.
She Dragon, Lion, Bore and Bull, she Heart and Hidra killed,
She Gereon, Cerberus, Angeus have, the birds and Centaries spild.
She paste Cocitus frostie face, with fixed faith to zoile,
And sailed Letheus Loathsome lake, forgettyng foes to foil.
She waded fiery Phlegeton flud, with princely patience out,
And stayed not at Stigias stream, to end her Pilgrim out:
She from Lotophagus then retournd, she past the Ciclops dean,
She sailed safe by Scillas rock, she fled not Auernus fene.
No Gorgone grimme, no Ciclop huge, no fretyng furies fell,
No Cirses sight, no Cirens voice nor Plutos force in hell:
May move her Mast nor shake her Ship upon a rock so build,
On Acheronta where she sails toward Elizeus filde.
Enioiyng Sanges glisteryng Gemmes, possessing Tagus sands,
Hidaspes and Hiberus wealth, with Pactolus pearls in hands:
The buildinges brave and lofty wales, the princely Turrets hie,
Which Ninus wife in Persea plast, whose fame shall never die.
The sumptuous Tomb, the wondrous works, the gorgious glistryng grave,
Which Queen of Caria then did make, Monsolus bones to save:
The stately seats Piramydes, which in Aegipt land abode,
And Monstrous Phoebus shape I say, which Chares made in Rhode.
Dianas Temple Pharaos Toure, and Phidias frame in Greece,
In spect of this her buildings brave, their fame and glory lose:
Cadmus Thebes, Dardane Troie, and Ogdous Memphus made,
These with Carthage Didos Tonne, may nothing here persuade.
She builds New Jerusalem, and Sion up does rear,
And to the Vineyard on her back she daielie burdens bear:
The fletyng fame on earth have sought, she glory great in sky,
They through pomp and she through pain aduaunceth fame on hi^.
They liude to eat, they eat to die, they die in death to pain,
She eats to live she lives to die, she dies to live again:
Ariadne Minoes daughter taught, to Theseus stout the way,
From Labirinthus dreadful deans, where Minotaurus lay.
That wandryng Prince Aeneas then, in Cuma taught was so,
By sage Amalthea Auernus gulf, from limbo lake to go:
The soundrie shifts Medea assaide, her Jason sure to save,
In Colcus land with conquest great, the golden fleace to have.
Were now these worthy Greeks alive, and iolie champions lo,
As unto Delphos streigt they would unto his palace go:
She spoiled Labirinthus caves, she Minotaurus slew,
She princely does possess in place, the Golden fleace in vewe.
And though Perillus played his part, the brazen Bull to make,
Yet was Perillus forced him self, therein his death to take.
The Leaden horse Aruntius made, for others there to die,
To assaie the new invented work, Aruntius first did fry.
Thus saucie Sinon slight shall die, and Phalaris falshode end,
And from Zopirus subtle seed, her state shall God defend.
Triumphant Triton sound thy trumpe, though Neptunes rage did rore,
In spite of Boreas Boisterous blast, she sailed saffe to shore.
Where she like blasyng Phoebus beams, with silver shinyng face,
Bellona like in seat does sit, with fine Aglaias grace.
On whom the dreadful Driades wait, the Oriades high on hill,
The sacred Nymphs attend her grace, her service to fulfill.
Hebe and Calisto light, from starrie states discende,
A perles prince must pearce the clouds, whose fame the skies shall bend.
Whose fame shall flow from Nilus banks, through Euphrates to land:
From Tigris stragglyng stream, her state to golden Ganges sand.
From Pirens pathe to Helicon, from high Parnassus mount,
Unto Libethres well to wash with ladies nine were wont.
From East to Weste, from Southe to North, her fliyng fame does flow,
From Pole antartike fast with wings, to articke Pole to go.
Panomphes Gemme Caliope, thy step Melpomen stay,
Yield honor due Thalia now, her service Clio assaie.
Polihimnia with her Music must, and Errato tend in place,
Euterpe sound her troumpe of fame, her foes for to deface.
Discende Terpsichore from the skies, part thence Urania fro,
A Phoenix fine must mount the skies, and you away must go.
Surrender Cumea up thy seaete, now Samia Phiton flee,
No longer fame must Themis have, in Delphos now to be.
Yield stately Juno Samos up, flee Delos Dian fro,
Void princely Pallas Athens now, from Paphos Venus go.
Behold a branche from Brutus line, whose virtuous praise to paint,
My slender skill, my simple muse to think thereof do faint.
For had I Virgills vain in verse, and learned Homere skill,
To write Demosthenes sugared style, with noble Tullies quill.
Ouercharged I should be, her noble life to cite,
Her galaunt gifts, her talentes rare, her virtuous all to write.
If outward beauty moude the Gods, what inward virtue can,
Whose feutered form if eyes might see, should maze the sight of man,
If Daphnes might Apollo win, and Tyro Neptune move,
If Herce Cecrops daughter dear, might lure Mercurius love.
If Queen Aeginas beauty bright, could mighty Jove inflame,
In form of fire with Phoebus face, to honor beauties fame.
What love, what joy, what wisdom then, what wealth should men possess,
If sense of sight might virtue see, as Plato prince confess.
The tramplyng toil of Tantalus, his hongrie panche to please,
The rowlyng stone of Sisiphus, her virtuous sight would cease.
If Titius could, if Atlas might, might this Yxion see,
Titius toil Atlas load, Yxion easde should be.
The golden hears Pompeia had, forede Neros heart on fire,
Through wit and wisdom did our Queen, her subjects hearts desire.
Cleopatra wanne by a draught of drink, Antonius for her friend,
By counsel grave and sad advise, does she her zoile defend.
The curious shue made Rhodope Queen, in Egipte land to rule,
And virtue aduaunceth this her fame, in England long to endure.
But sith that beauty is a gift, which nature has bestowed.
I mean to pen dame beauties praise, as nature forth has showed.
Had skillful eyes of Xeuxes vued, had fine Philuseus seen,
Had learned ^o Lisippus sight, of this our noble Queen.
Dame Venus picture made in Cous, and Helens grace in Greece,
Latonas moulde, the impes of Jove, their painted port should leese.
If Phidias might had framde the like, or had Timan^es fainde,
Bellona in her bower had blushed, and I phigenia stainde.
Durst this Pigmalion grave in stone, or Theopompus painte,
They should have felt both sight and sense, both heart and hand to fain^
If prince Apelles cunnyng, could in colors draw by art,
The sight thereof would cause the Greeks from such a sh^we to starte.
Brag then no more you glorious Greeks, of beauties bower ne boste,
Sith England wanne victoriously, which Greece by fate has lost.
Retire you Romans from your race, your fame is lost I wene,
Such Lucrece reign in England now, as never erst was seen.
Give place you noble dames, throw of your garlandes gaie,
Lay prostrate down your crounes of fame, to her that bears the swaie.
Who in spite of dragging darte of death, in spite of Gorgons grief,
In spite of furies frettyng flames, in spite of Parcas knife.
Shall Triton sound her troumpe of fame, and Stentor strain to blow,
Through surging seas and clousteryng clouds, her name to stars to go.
And in Mauseolum brave her bones, and sumptuous Toumbe shall lie,
Whose sacred souls berefte from corpse, to heaven straight shall fly.
FINIS.
The Aucthour to his book.
Since Virgill lofty vain in verse, since learned Homers Quill,
Since Plutarch and Pithagoras, did practice often their skill:
To write of fightyng frogs, in filled of wandering bees and flice,
To extol the reeds and rushes long, with solemn muse advice.
Though skillful they with learned muse, in things so small to flow,
Yet willing I my faithful heart to native zoill to show:
For who in Athens idle were, by Dracos law should die,
As in Hetruria idle heads, by law were forcde to fly.
Whose ground in Rome was baren found, was put in prison fast,
And king of Egypt took account, of daily labor past:
The Spartan youth that waxed gross by law should fast it out,
And loitring Indians went vndinde, from place to place about.
Each labor well deserves hire, that Idleness does flee,
Each virtue must have due reward, each vice must punished be:
For rather had I venture fame, Chirilus stripes to have,
Then still with Vatia idle be, each filthy lust to laue.
Saill then by raging Scillas rocks, pass by Ciclops den,
On through Lotophagos tent full bold, fear not Auernus fene,
Trudge fast from flattryng Cirens face, make haste from Cirses sight,
Pearce Limbo through, possess the place where justice judges right.
Mauger of Maegera head, in spit of furies go,
Through Stigia stream and flaming fluds to fre^d thy self to show
Way not Momus nor his mates, exteme not Zoilus talk,
Void fond Suffenus busy brain, with wise Minerua walk.
The Scithian rude had rather here, a horse to ney or kike,
Then solemn sound of Orpheus Harp, with skillful stroke to strike
The Arcadian blind with Midas will, prefer the pipe of Pan,
Before Appollo sacred God, who judge of music can.
Had thou Briareus hundred hands, or hundred Argos eyes,
Suppose thy senses slack to serve, each reader to suffice:
And as Vlixes mates were changde, to shiftyng shapes of hoggs,
So like Actaeon thou shall be, devoured of some dogs.
Some will find in vearse thy fault, some will defame thy sense,
Some thy style and some thy state, will find in thee offense:
The learned loath I am to lose, of right their friendship crave,
Since thou and they were taught in place, true friendship seek to have.
A Crow sometime in Rome was taught, to speak to Caesar so,
Aue Caesar loud with voice, as Caesar by did go:
An Aegle was in Seston town, each day would never cease,
But mount to skey in flight to slay, some fowls his friend to please.
The Pecock proud the swellyng Swan, the Feasaunt gaie the Cock,
Do brag and bost themselves so brave, and other birds do mock:
Though these like Gorgons grim with eyes, thy tre^bling face behold
Yet tell that others see in them, which they of thee have toulde.
No force say thou if that may chance, Antimachus lot may crave,
In steed of sondrie diverse heads, one Plato friend to have.

OF the slipperie state of Fortune, and what Princes, and where they were
advanced one way, and how they were oppressed an other way. Fol. 1. page 1.

sumptuous and wonderful buildings. fol. 20. pa. 1. Of Paintyng. Fol. 23. page
1. Of eloquence. Fol, 25. pagi. 1. Of those that had their Pictures and Images,
for a show of their deserved fame, erected. Fol* 28. page 1. Of those that
defended diverse from deaths, from serpents, dragons, and of cunnyng archers.
fol. 30. pa. 2. Of diligence and labors. Fol. 32. page 2. Of the first
inuentours of arts, and of the use of sothsaiyng. Fol. 35. page 1. Of
